KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
GOVERNANCE STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT CREDIT
CREDIT SUMMARY
Borrower:       Kyrgyz Republic
Amount:         SDR 14.7 million (US$ 20 million equivalent)
Terms:          IDA terms with a 40 year maturity, including a grace period of 10 years.
Objectives:     The Governance Structural Adjustment Credit (GSAC) would support the Government's
program to strengthen the effectiveness of the State. It has two complementary objectives:
(a) to improve the transparency and responsiveness of the public sector and enhance the
ability of external stakeholders to hold it accountable; and (b) to increase efficiency,
effectiveness and accountability within the public sector. With regard to the first objective,
the GSAC would support reforms aimed at (i) improving personal accountability of
political officials and civil servants; (ii) strengthening voice and participation by improving
public access to official information; (iii) improving the interaction between regulatory
agencies and business enterprises; and (iv) strengthening external audit. To advance the
second objective, the operation would support reforms directed at (i) strengthening public
expenditure management, by establishing a more strategic and transparent budget
formulation process, and strengthening accountability and transparency in budget
execution; (ii) improving the internal control environment; (iii) improving transparency,
value for money and accountability in public procurement; (iv) improving service delivery
in the health and education sectors; (v) enhancing the effectiveness of the civil service; and
(vi) streamlining the structure of Government ministries and agencies.
Benefits:       The GSAC would increase the ability of citizens, civil society and the private sector to
demand improvements in State performnance. This would result from enhanced access to
information about key aspects of public sector operations and increased participation in
State activities. In parallel, the program would strengthen core competencies within the
public sector. Reforms in public expenditure management would strengthen the linkage
between policies and public spending, better align resource availability and responsibilities
at the lower levels of Government and improve efficiency, predictability and accountability
in budget execution. Reforms in public procurement would improve cost-effectiveness of
government spending on goods and services. Increased professionalism and capacity of
the civil service would provide much needed skills in critical areas of policy formulation
and execution. Finally, streamlining of the structure of Government would result in
improved administrative efficiency and coordination. On the whole, by simultaneously
strengthening the voice of external stakeholders and improving the effectiveness of the
Government machinery, the operation would enhance the ability of the State to pursue the
objectives of the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS).
While reducing corruption in the Kyrgyz public sector would require sustained, long-term
efforts on many fronts, the GSAC would contribute to this cause in several ways. Some of
the reforms covered by the operation, such as publication of declarations of incomes and
assets, creation of administrative mechanisms to address grievances of citizens and the
private sector and strengthening of disciplinary rules applicable to civil servants, have a
direct anti-corruption objective. Others would address the problem indirectly through
systemic institutional reforms that increase transparency and accountability. For instance,
increased awareness of State activities and relevant rules and regulations would enable
external stakeholders to demand higher standards of conduct and integrity from public



Kyrgyz Republi: Governance Strucht ral Adjustinent Credit - Program Document, Apria 21, 2003
officials. Similarly, enhanced civic part6cipation in the budget process; reduced cliscretion
and informality in budget execution resulting from modemization of treasury operations;
strengthening of internal co ritrol and external auclit functions; and increased transparency
in the procurement process would diminish opportunities for fraud and corruption in public
expenditure management. Finally, the eliminaticin of financing mechanisrns that create
perverse incentives for regulatory agenc ies would reduce the tendency of these agencies to
misuse authority.
Risks:          Improvements in public sector petfomiance take a long time to materialize and are
vulnerable to opposition from vested interests. Strong, sustained political commitmnent is
therefore absolutely necessary. Waning of political support can significantly slow or even
derail the rnforn process. 'I'he lirmited institutional capacity of the Government also poses
a serious risk. Fuither, the proposed refomis involve a large number of GovemrrLent
agencies, stakeholders and clonors. Deficiencies in coordination between these actors
could also seriously affect implementation. In order to mitigate these risks, the
government's own reform agenda, as spelled out in the NPRS, has been made the core of
the GSAC reforms, so as ensure ownership. The operation has required the Government tc,
undertake several measures before Boar(d presenlation, to demonstrate its comrnmitment to
reform. Also disbursements have been back-loaded. Significant technical assistance will
be provided to augment the Government's capacity. A broad- based project cooidination
and management structure has been created to ensure active involvement of relevant
stakeholders and close coordination of different activities. Notwithstanding these measures,
the overall risk of the operation will r.emain high This is due to the inherently difficult
nature of institutional and governance reforms, which are subject to a number of, difficult
to control, political, social and economic: variables. The high risk, is however, matched by
potentially high rewards.

Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003
PROGRAM DOCUMENT
OF THE INTERNATIONAL DEVELOPMENT ASSOCIATION
TO THE EXECUTIVE DIRECTORS
ON A PROPOSED GOVERNANCE STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT CREDIT TO
THE KYRGYZ REPUBLIC
1.    I submit for your approval the following Program Document on a proposed Governance
Structural Adjustment Credit (GSAC) to the Kyrgyz Republic for the equivalent of SDR 14.7 million
(US$ 20 million). The proposed Credit is the eighth in a series of adjustment operations designed to
support the Government's overall program of stabilization and structural reform.
I.     INTRODUCTION
2.     The Kyrgyz Republic has carried out significant reforms in economic policy since independence.
However, reforms in governance and public sector management have not kept pace with the policy
reforns. Deficiencies in these areas bave emerged as major constraints in the ability of the country to
fight poverty. Accordingly, the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS) gives a high priority to
improving the effectiveness of the State. It outlines a multi-dimensional strategy for this purpose,
covering reforms in democratic institutions, law making processes, the judiciary and law enforcement
agencies, public administration, public expenditure management, civil service and local self Government.
3.     Some of the reforms included in the NPRS fall outside the World Bank's mandate, while some
others are being assisted by other development partners. The proposed Governance Structural
Adjustment Credit (GSAC), would focus on institutional reforms, primarily in the Executive branch of
the State. The GSAC would have two complementary objectives: (a) to improve the transparency and
responsiveness of the public sector and enhance the ability of external stakeholders to hold it accountable;
and (b) to increase efficiency, effectiveness and accountability within the public sector.
4.    It is recognized that achieving significant, sustainable improvements in governance and public
sector performance in the Kyrgyz Republic will be a long term endeavor. The public administration in
the country is still at the initial stages of development. The capacity of the Govemment to implement
reforms is limited. Also, institutional reforms in public sector management are intrinsically complex and
difficult, as the World Bank's own record in this area indicates. Therefore, the effectiveness of the State
would have to be built up step by step, through iterative reforms spread over many years.
5.     The Government has taken several initial steps in the last few years. These include
implementation of first generation reforms in public expenditure management under the World Bank
financed Public Sector Resource Management Adjustment Credit (PSRMAC), creation of a Chamber of
Accounts, adoption of a modern Public Procurement Law, enactment of a Civil Service Law,
retrenchment of staff at the central level, reduction in the number of ministries and State bodies,
launching of a concerted action plan to remove administrative barriers to investment, establishment of
elected local self Governments, implementation of major reforms in the health sector, increasing public
access to information about the functioning of the State and efforts to encourage participation of citizens
and civil society in public affairs. Annex 2 provides details of the some of the reforms implemented in
the recent past. The GSAC represents a continuation of the reform process. In the areas covered by it,
the program is expected to deepen the reforms that have already been initiated and make incremental
improvements in the capability of the State. It would also prepare the ground for more advanced reforms
in the future.
6.     While the experience of the Government in implementing the first order reforms will be
extremely helpful, significant technical assistance (TA) would be needed to successfully implement the



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjushnent Credit - Program Document,, April .21, 2003  2
GSAC program. Important grant-based TA would be provided for many activities by other donors,
including the DFID, USAID, TJNDP, IMF and EU-TACIS. TA for the remaining activities will be
provided through the Bank financed Governance Technical Assistance Credit (GTAC), which
accompanies the GSAC.
7.     The GSAC would be disbursed in three tranches: SDR 3.67'i million (US$ 5 million equivalent)
upon Effectiveness , SDR 3.675 million (US$ in million ecluivalent) after satisfaction of second tranche
release conditions (expected in first/second quarter of 2004e), and SDR 7.350 million (US$ 10 millionl
equivalent) after satisfaction of third tranche release conditions (expected in first/second quarter of 2005).
II. TIHE ECONOMY
A.     Macroeconomic Performance and Policy Framewiork
8.     Macroeconomic Performance: The K:yrgyz Republic is a small, land-locked country with a.
population of 4.9 million. Agriculture accounts for about 4() percent of GDP. Gold and electricity are the
two most important exports. Following independence, income levels declined by about 60 percent due to
discontinuance of budgetary transfers from the Soviet Unionr, disruption of traditional trading
relationships, terms of trade shocks and a 50 percent cumulative decline in GDP between 1991 and 1995.
9.     The country has undertaken systemic reforms since 1993 that have helped create the basic
foundations of a market-oriented economic syst-m consisting, inter alia, of a market-based price system;
an open, foreign trade regime; and substantive Fprivate ownership of key productive assets. The Kyrgyz
Republic was the first in the CIS to join the W1"O. The econorric reforms contributed to GDP growth in
1996 (7.1 percent) and 1997 (9.9 percent). A large number of small and medium enterprises emerged
during this period. The regional financial crisis in 1998, however, halted economic recovery. It also
highlighted continuing vulnerabilities caused ba large financial imbalances in the energy sector, fragile
public finances, inefficient safety nets and a poorly developed banking sector.
10.   Although these weaknesses have not been fully addressed, the country has made significant
progress in attaining macroeconomic stability anid restarting economi c growth. The fiscal deficit fell from
about 11.3 percent of GDP in 1998 to an estimated 5.2 percent of GDP in 2002, mostly as a consequence
of expenditure cuts - primarily, externally financed public investment expenditures and, to a lesser extent,
social transfers. Similarly, efforts have been made to reduce quasi-fiscal deficits since 2000 by steadily
increasing power sector tariffs above inflation and taking complementary measures in the sector. The
current account deficit declined from an average of 18.4 percent of GrDP (in 1996-98) to 3.2 percent of
GDP in 2002. As a result of the fiscal and monetary tightening, inflation fell to 2.3 percent in 2002. The
debt service-flow relief from the Paris Club in early-2001 hlas also helped authorities move forward wvith
the ongoing fiscal adjustment process.
1l.   Despite the disruptions from the regional financial crisis, growth has averaged about 4.8 percent
in 1999-2001. Although significant disturbance!s from landslides at the Kumtor gold mine and the
reduced external demand for power in 2002 inte-rrupted this progress - with growth rate estimated to turn
slightly negative in 2002 - growth is expected to return to its positive trend from 2003 onwards, barring
any unexpected shocks. The economy's production base continues to be narrowly defined, but the
spending patterns entered into a more viable path. On the supp]ly side of the economy, growth has been
led by agriculture and two major, non-labor intensive industrial sectors, i.e. gold mining and power, with
first signs of recovery in manufacturing performance over t he last decade being signaled only in 2002.
On the demand side, it is encouraging that the recent adjustmrrents in the fiscal deficit and the external
account deficit have resulted in a more sustainable stance, with an increase in domestic savings.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003               3
12.     These favorable developments notwithstanding, the country continues to face serious challenges.
The Kyrgyz Republic remains the second poorest of the FSU republics and one of the poorest countries in
the world. In 2001, per capita income was less than US$300. Severe poverty affected 13.5 percent of the
population and 47.6 percent were considered poor, having incomes below PPP US$4.30 per day.
Poverty is highest in rural areas, but there are large regional disparities. Access to public services such as
running water, public sewerage, reliable electricity, district heating, and telephone service is very low.
Most of the population is vulnerable to poverty because of the low savings levels and the absence of
financial instruments to allow for consumption smoothing.
13.     Notwithstanding the fiscal adjustment, the country is saddled with a heavy burden of external
debt - at 477 percent of Government revenues, in net present value terms, at end-2001. The Paris Club
Agreement did not change the stock of external debt, although the agreement includes a goodwill clause
that allows for a potential stock reduction operation in the future. There are also issues relating to the
quality and sustainability of fiscal adjustment efforts to date. Adjustment in recent years has been
achieved largely on the basis of across-the-board expenditure cuts that are unsustainable and have often
failed to reflect strategic priorities. Civil servants are poorly paid and often seek alternative sources of
income, which reduces the quality of public services and promotes rent-seeking behavior. Reduced
maintenance of infrastructure has affected such critical areas as irrigation networks, transportation and
education and health facilities. Public expenditures on social protection, other than pensions, have been
severely constrained. Finally, the Government is still incurring substantial quasi-fiscal expenditures,
estimated to be over 10 percent of GDP in 2001, by pricing public utility services below their economic
costs.
14.     Despite the Kyrgyz Republic's liberal trade regime and WTO membership, international trade has
so far failed to boost growth'and productivity. The country is dependent on its neighbors for access to
markets. None of them, except China, has, however, joined the WTO. They also have inconsistent trade
policies and'place various restrictions on flow of goods from the Kyrgyz Republic. Uncertainties in
regional trade and transit arrangements have discouraged foreign direct investment in the country, which
has averaged only 2.2 percent of the GDP since 1996. There are also well-documented internal barriers to
investment, including excessive regulation, through permits, certification, licensing and inspections, and
absence of basic financial services and credit.
15.     Macroeconomic Policy Framework: Maintaining macroeconomic stability and growth, reducing
external debt and achieving further gains in poverty reduction requires continuation of fiscal and external
adjustment; further progress in structural reforms; and continued support from external creditors, both in
the form of further debt rescheduling and increased grant and concessional financing. A 3-year
arrangement under the Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility (PRGF), approved by the Executive Board
of the IMF in December 2001, provides the basic underpinnings for this policy framework'. The
implementation of the PRGF program has thus far remained on track and the specific targets established
under the program have generally been met, and even exceeded in some key areas.
' The PRGF program targets a reduction in the fiscal deficit of the general govemment, consistent with the balance of payments adjustment
needed to address the country's external debt problems. The key elements of this effort are an increase in collections of tax revenue through a
combination of measures that include, a broadening of the tax base and improvements in tax administration led by the establishment of a modem
Large Taxpayer Unit, as well as expenditure restraint. The PRGF program also requires the pursuance of a debt strategy, including seeking debt
relief from Paris Club creditors on Naples terms, a reduction in the size of the Public Investment Program, and limting external borrowing to
loans with high levels of concessionality. The program anticipates the implementation of a tight monetary pohcy in support of low inflation and
a stable nominal exchange rate Structural reforms in the PRGF program focus on governance and financial sctor reforms. As regards
govemance, the PRGF identifies measures in the areas of institution building, public awareness, efficiency of public administration, corporate
governance andjudicial reforms. In this regard, it particularly emphasizes the restructuring of the Ministry of Finance as a key step to improve
the administration of public finances. In the financial sector, reforms of bank supervision, improvements of the regulatory framework, the
enhancement of debt recovery capacity, the divestiture of state-owned banks, and the development of the payments system are key pnonties.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Progrmn Document,, April 2J, 2003  4
16.    The Government's reform efforts have Elso been supported through adjustment lending by the
World Bank. Seven Bank adjustment operations supported privatization and enterprise reform,
agricultural privatization, financial sector refonr, public sector resource management, pension reformn and
power and gas sector reform along with imnprove ments in thle business environment. These operations
helped the Kyrgyz Republic undertake reforms to orient its policy and institutional framework to market
economy principles, although reforms are far from complete. The proposed Governance Structural
Adjustment Credit (GSAC) and the accomnpanying Governance Technical Assistance Credit will broaden
and deepen the progress made so far in public sector reform. These cperations complement both the
PRGF program and the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS).
B.     Macroeconomic Prospects
17.    Prospects for Growth: In view of the country's low income and high poverty levels, achieving
sustainable growth is the most fundamental requirement for progress in poverty reduction. A
considerable further adjustment both in external and fiscal accounts will need to underpin these efforts.
Public investment and consumption-led growth of 1996-01, financed primarily by external resources, is
no longer affordable over the medium tern. Along with redlucing the reliance on external financing,
increased access to grants and concessional financing are critical to the sustainability of this adjustme-nt.
In addition, a greater effort is needed to shift the origins of the externil imbalance from public investrnent
to private sector activities. While growth performance since 2000 was comparatively more balanced,
private investment and export performance are still far from iproviding tangible growth stimulus.
18.    Clearly, a more vibrant and outward-oriented private sector will be critical to maintaining the
momentum of growth, broadening its base and increasing the economy's access to diversified inflows of
extemal capital, including foreign direct investment. In the period ahead, diminishing gold reserves in the
Kumntor gold mine are already expected to make it more difficult to sustain exports and growth. The
reforms in the agricultural sector, if broadened and deepened to create an enabling environment for
private sector initiatives and to induce productivity gains, are likely to play a major role in maintaining a
positive trend in export and overall economic growth. Similar efforts are needed for improving the
investment climate for SMEs, which is key to ermiployment ge:neration and diversification of sources of
growth. In addition, it is necessary to attract private investments in sectors where the econonmic potential
of the Kyrgyz Republic is significant, such as hydro-generation as well as mining.
19.    These efforts will need to be complemenited by a coherent trade sector promotion strategy, with
an emphasis on trade facilitation and diversification. The country's geographical location implies that:
improvement in the regional trade environment is equally essential for its future growth prospects. Trade
barriers imposed by the neighboring countries, especially during the period of the regional financial crisis,
have had a considerable negative impact on the country's exports. An anticipated improvement in the
regional trade environment over the medium-term through increase in demand from the neighboring
countries as well as gradual progress in their integration into the WTCO are essential to the country's future
growth prospects. Continued public finance and governance reforms will also be needed to underpin
efforts to broaden the base of growth over the medium-tenn (see Section mII for detailed discussion).
20.    As reflected in their NPRS, the authorities have pledged them,elves to press forward with their
economic reform programs. Under a scenario of continued policy refonn, simulations indicate that
maintaining a per capita growth rate of over 3 percent and reducing the fiscal deficit below 4 percent of
GDP over the medium term would be consistent with a steady improvement of the debt burden in the
period ahead. However, reduction of the external debt burden to sustainable levels through this decade
would be possible only if the ongoing efforts to maintain access to corcessional external financing anci to
secure a concessional debt stock reduction are successful. It should be emphasized that improving the
depth and pace of structural reforms and fiscal capacity over the medium-term is critical for maintaining



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003            5
access to such strong donor assistance and the realization of full benefits of reforms. Falling short of this
target may result in a forced domestic and external adjustment, without addressing the fundamental
weaknesses of the economy. As such, it would have a negative impact on growth prospects, would re-
generate fiscal pressures and could undermine the hard-won gains toward poverty alleviation. (see
Country Assistance Strategy, Attahment 1, for detailed elaboration on extemal and fiscal debt
sustainability analysis).

III. THE GOVERNMENT'S PROGRAM TO INCREASE
THE EFFECTIVENESS OF THE STATE
21.    The quality of governance will be a key determinant of the success of the Kyrgyz Republic in
achieving sustainable growth and poverty reduction. The State would need to play a major role in the
implementation of the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS). It would have to develop pro-poor
policies that maintain macro-economic stability; spur sustainable growth; protect the vulnerable;
encourage the emergence of a vibrant private sector; and promote a free, open and just society, where
citizens have the opportunity to achieve their potential. The State would also need to muster the requisite
financial, human and physical resources needed to put the policies into effect. Finally, it would have to
ensure that the policies are implemented effectively and their benefits actually reach the poor. To
discharge these responsibilities, the State would need to have the requisite technical and administrative
capacity. But even more important, it would need to operate in a manner that inspires trust and galvanizes
the people around the goal of poverty reduction. This would be possible only if the State is focused on
promoting the interests of the poor, its policies are formulated and executed in a transparent manner, the
voice of the people is heard and the agents of the State are held accountable for their actions.
22.    As already mentioned, in recent years, the Government has taken various steps to strengthen the
institutional capacity of the State. However, there is still a long way to go. The NPRS provides a candid
diagnosis of the problems that continue to limit the effectiveness of the State. At the political level, the
electoral system needs to be improved, political parties are weak and the communication between the
State and the people is not fully effective. The legal framework is still in the process of development and
suffers from outdated norms, high frequency of change, lack of public discussion of proposed laws and
regulations and inadequate public access to normative acts. The judiciary and law enforcement agencies
face serious capacity and integrity challenges that reduce their ability to uphold the law. At the level of
central and local administration, policy making capacity is extremely weak, implementation of policies is
uneven, transparency and accountability are inadequate and Government interference in business
activities is excessive. Local self-Governments lack adequate functional and fiscal authority,
management capacity and community participation. Public expenditure management reforms need to be
deepened to strengthen budget formulation, budget execution and financial accountability. Further
improvements are needed in the system of inter-Governmental finance. The level of political interference
and instability in the civil service remains high, while its professional capacity and accountability for
performance remain low. The structure of Government needs to be rationalized. Corruption is a major
systemic problem that plagues many public agencies, as confirmed by surveys of households and firms
(Annex 6 summnarizes of the key findirngs of the surveys).
23.    The Government recognizes that weaknesses of the State will pose a serious hurdle in the
country's development path and in its effort to alleviate poverty. For this reason, the NPRS accords a
very high priority to the formation of an effective State and includes a multi-faceted strategy, sketched in
Figure 1, below and summarized in Annex 7.

24.         For the purpose of the GSAC, the Government has selected a subset of high priority reforms that
are indicated in italics in Figure 1. Reforms of the judiciary and tax and customs administrations, though
important, were not included in the GSAC program in order to avoid overloading the operation. Some of
the political reforms proposed under the NPRS fall outside the Bank's mandate. Some of the other
reforms are being supported by other donors2.
25.         The reforms supported by the GSAC fall under two mutually reinforcing categories. First,
refonns designed to improve the transparency and responsiveness of the public sector and enhance the
2 In addition to the reforms supported by the IMF's PRGF mentioned above (see Footnote 1), the Asian Development Bank is supporting

DFID have been assisting the Govemment's efforts to streamline the structure of govemment ministries and agencies through functional reviews.
The USAID is supporting regulatory and administrative reform initiatives to improve the business environment.

ability of external stakeholders to hold it accountable. Second, reforms aimed at increasing efficiency,
effectiveness and accountability within the public sector. Specific actions proposed to be taken under
these categories, discussed in detail below, would operate to (a) improve rules and restraints; (b)
strengthen voice and participation; and (c) increase competitive pressures, as illustrated in Figure 2.
These actions are expected to gradually realign incentives within the public sector and build the State's
capability to play its expected role in the achievement of the country's strategic objectives.

26.    The Govenmment realizes that strengthening the effectiveness of the State will be a long process,
requiring sustained efforts spread of over several years. This is due to the inherent complexity of
institutional reforms that seek to bring about behavioral changes, the large number of exogenous variables
that could affect progress and the evidently weak institutional capacity of the State at present. Therefore,
improvements in governance will be realized only slowly and incrementally. Yet, given the centrality of
good governance to development and poverty reduction, it is imperative to undertake the next steps in the
reform process. Also, due to the inter-linkages between different areas of public administration, it is
necessary to move forward along several dimensions at the same time. The proposed reformns are seen as



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structrral Adjustnent Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  9
important, but modest next steps that will build on recent initiatives and would, in turn, be complemented
by future interventions. With regard to feasibility of implementing the proposed reforms, the Government
is of the view that it has already implemented the first phase of most of the reforms included in the
program. Efforts have been made to keep the level of sophistication of the reforms within achievable
limits. As such, the Government is reasonably confident that, given adequate technical assistance, it
would be able to implement the second generation reformns.
A.     IMPROVE THE TRANSPARENCY AND RESPONSIVENESS OFTHE PUBLIC
SECTOR AND ENHANCE THE ABILITY OF EXTERNAL STAKEHOLDERS
TO HOLD IT ACCOUNTABLE
(i)    Improving Personal Accountability of Political Officials and Civil Servants
27.    One of the instruments frequently used to promote integrity amongst political leaders and civil
servants who exercise State authority, is to require public officials to periodically declare their own and
their close relatives' incomes and assets and make their declarations public. The obligation to file the
declarations makes it harder for politicians and bureaucrats to hide gains from corruption; imposes an
implicit obligation to explain sources of investment in property; and creates potentially incriminating
evidence that could be used against them in future corruption investigations. At the same time, the
publication of the declarations provides the media, civil society and the public with useful benchmarks
against which they can measure the actual life styles of public officials and demand explanations for
observed discrepancies. By increasing the likelihood of detection of corruption, the system dissuades
many public officials from venturing into it. By providing documentary evidence of corruption, it
facilitates punishing those who persist.
28.    Currently, the Election Code, the Law on KR Jogorku Kenesh Deputies' Status and Civil Service
Law3 have some provisions for declaration of income and assets by candidates, deputies to Parliament and
civil servants, respectively, but these need to be improved and expanded. None of the laws requires
publication of the declarations. The declarations filed by Deputies and civil servants are explicitly treated
as secret. Also, these officials are not required to file any declarations regarding the income and assets of
their close family members. The State Revenue Committee, which receives the declarations of deputies
and civil servants does not have the capacity to systematically scrutinize these to identify cases of illicit
enrichment.
29.    The Government is keenly interested in increasing the accountability of persons in authority, in
order to combat corruption. For this purpose, it proposes to improve the effectiveness of the system of
declaration of income and assets. The Government has issued decrees indicating its intention to submit
to Parliament (a) a draft Law on Declaration and Publication of Income and Assets of High State Officials
in the Executive, Judicial and Legislative Branches of the State, including the President, Prime Minister,
Vice Prime Ministers, Ministers, Deputies Ministers, Judges and members of the Parliament and their
close family members; and (b) amendments to the Civil Service Law, satisfactory to IDA, to (i) require
declaration of income and assets of civil servants and their close family members and (ii) make the
declarations of incomes and assets of civil servants available to the public, and declarations of incomes
and assets of their close family members available to specified state bodies. Before second tranche
release, the Law on Declaration and Publication of Income and Assets of High State Officials and the
aforesaid amendments to the Civil Service Law will be enacted. Before third tranche, the Government
will ensure publication of declarations of income and assets of High State Officials and their close family
3Article 27 of the Election Code of the Kyrgyz Republic (April 29, 1999); Article 37 of the Law on KR Jogorku Kenesh Deputies Status and
Article 13, the Law on Civil Service, 1999.
4Italicized text in this section indicates actions that are conditions of release of the second and third tranches.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Sftructurzl Adjustment Credit-- Program D'ocinzenF,, April 21, 2003  10
members, in accordance with the aforesaid La-zv. It will also make the declarations of income and assets
of civil servants available, on request, to the putblic5. Appropriate administrative arrangements, to ensure
regular, effective examination of the declarations of civil servants and their close family members will be
implemented to detect conflict of interest and illicit enrichmenet.
(fi)   Strengthening Voice and Participation by Improving Access to Official Information.
30.    The availability of relevant and timely information about the functioning of the public sector is an
important pre-requisite for enabling civil society, the media and the public to contribute to policy
dialogue, participate in consultative processes and oversee the performance of public sector entities. The
Kyrgyz Republic has a relatively open Govermnent and is making e Fforts to increase the transparency of
the public sector and increase public engagement in Governmental activities. For example, public budget
hearings were held to receive feedback on the annual budget for 2OC2. The budget and the Fiscal Bulletin
are published. Web sites of several Government entities have been created to disseminate informnation on
the internet. However, the penchant for secrecy still survives in several areas of Government and the
quality and accessibility of official information remains quite limited, as revealed by surveys of
households and enterprises.
31.    The NPRS places a high priority on strengthening civic participation in budget formulation and
execution at all levels of Government and in other decisions involving general community interests. It
also aims to increase the ability of civil society to monitor activities Af State agencies. For this purpose,
the Government plans to improve public access to information on activities of State agencies and local
authorities. In accordance with agreements wilh IDA, the government has already published annual
audited financial Statements and audit reports of 10 largest joint stock companies with State share of 51
percent or more, for FY 2001; information on public procurement contract awards exceeding $20,000
made in 2002; the complete annual audit report of the Chamber- of Accounts on the execution of the
republican budget for FY 2001, as adopted by the Parliament; the lisi: of firms receiving tax concessions
and exemptions, as an investment incentive; andc the list of 50 largest delinquent taxpayers and energy
users. It has also organized a workshop to disseminate the "Governance and Service Delivery" report
prepared by the World Bank, based on service delivery survey conducted in 2001. By second tranche, the
Government will institute mechanisms, satisfactory to IDA, for the regular publication of the documents
mentioned above, including information about the public sector reform program implementation and
results of ongoing monitoring and evaluation of the health sector reforms.
32.    Before third tranche release the Govermment will: (i) enact amendments to the Law on
Guarantees and Freedom ofAccess to Informaiion, satisfactory to IDA, to enhance access of the public to
official information, strengthen the mechanisms of individuals to appeal against denials of requests for
information, and provide a system for monitoring of compliance of state bodies with the Law. It will also
establish a Government Public Information Center to provide free access to official information of public
interest and to the portal and websites of the Government.
(iii)  Improving the Interaction between Regulatory Agencies and Business Enterprises
33.    The creation of a competitive business environment is one of cornerstones of the growth and
poverty reduction strategy of the Kyrgyz Republic. However, the interaction between the State and
enterprises suffers from various problems that dampen the development of the private sector.
34.    Many regulatory agencies are partially financed by 'special means funding', which includes fees
from licenses, permits, certificates and fines levied for infraction of regulations. This creates incentives
5 Italicized text in this section indicates actions that are conditions of release of the secon d and third tranches.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  1 1
-for regulatory excesses. To prevent misuse of regulatory and enforcement powers, through a Presidential
Decree, the Govemment has taken a decision to prohibit formnation and use of special means funding of
Governmental institutions through receipts from issue of permits, licenses and certificates and fines
imposed through inspections, except as specifically allowed by law. License fees were eliminated from
special means funding from the fiscal year 2002. The Government has issued a decree to prohibit
forrnation and use of special means funding of Governmental institutions through fees from issue of
permits. A similar action will be taken, before second tranche, with regard to fees from issue of
certificates and receipts from imposition of fines resulting from inspections. Before second tranche, the
Government will execute the Budget on the basis that fees from issue of licenses, permits and other
similar requirements placed on economic activities, are directed only to the State budget and not credited
to the Special Means Accounts of regulatory agencies, except for the State Energy Agency (SEA) and the
National Communications Agency (NCA). By third tranche, fees from certificates and receipts from
imposition of fines resulting from inspections would also be directed to the State budget. Thus, by third
tranche the Government would have successfully implemented a set of legislative and other measures,
satisfactory to IDA, to ensure thatfees, from issue of licenses, permits, certificates and other similar
requirements placed on economic activities, and fines resulting from inspections are directed only to the
State budget and not credited to the Special Means Accounts of regulatory agencies, with the exception of
SEA and NCA, and that regulatory agencies are provided compensatory funding through the State
budget.
35.    On the nornative side, there is multiplicity of laws and regulations affecting businesses, many of
which are outdated. There is inadequate participation of the private sector in development of new laws
and regulations. Little assessment of the impact of new regulatory instruments on the business
environment is carried out before they are put into effect. This also encourages proliferation of
regulations. To rectify these deficiencies, the Government has formed an inter-departmental commission
in the Ministry of Justice to review proposals for improvement of legislation. Draft amendments to the
Law on Normative Legal Acts, requiring all draft nonnative legal acts regulating or otherwise restricting
entrepreneurial entities to be made public, publicily discussed, harmonized and studied for their
economic, social and budgetary impact before being enacted, have been submitted to Parliament. Before
second tranche release, these amendments to the Law on Normative Legal Acts will be enacted and by the
third tranche, its provisions will be fully implemented for all Acts, issued after the passage of the Law.
There will also be a requirement for publishing brief information about draft Acts proposed to be
enacted, in the state gazette. Furthermore, the Government will conduct Regulatory Impact Assessments
of three new Laws and three new regulations, to be agreed with IDA, in accordance with the new Law.
36.    With regard to enforcement of regulations, regulatory agencies have overlapping authority, as a
result of which businesses face multiple inspections by different agencies for similar purposes. Private
firms are often unaware of the regulatory norms applicable to them, as also their rights and obligations
when they are subjected to inspections by State agencies. The mechanisms for addressing grievances of
enterprises against abuse of authority by regulatory agencies are weak.
37.    To address these problems, before the second tranche, the Government will connect the database
of the Ministry of Justice, which contains draft laws, enacted laws, regulations and instructions, to the
Internet to make it publicly available. By third tranche, the Government will enact a Law on
Administrative Procedures, satisfactory to IDA, to, inter alia, institute specific procedures for internal
administrative review of all actions which impose a penalty or burden upon, or deny a request sought by a
citizen or legal entity, and provide the right of appeal to a court, after exhaustion of administrative
appeals. With regard to regulatory activities of specific agencies, by second tranche, the Government will
develop and implement a plan for rationalizing public health related inspections carried out by the
Sanitary and Epidemiology Services (SES) and for rationalizing the pharmaceutical inspections carried
out by the Pharmaceutical Control Laboratory, publicizing their rules, regulations and results, improving



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance StructurolAdjustment Credit- Program Docuim ent,, April21, 2003  12
oversight by the Ministry of Health and creating effective grievance mechanisms against regulatory
abuse. Also before second tranche, the Government will develop a plan for rationalization of the
functions and reorganization of Kyrgyzstandarcd, satisfactory to IDA. By third tranche, the Governrnent
will implement the plan for rationalization of tELe functions and reorganization of Kyrgyzstandard.
(iv)   Strengthening External Audit
38.    An effective, independent external audit is essential for enforcing accountability in the public
sector and enabling the Parliament to oversee the working of the executive. Significant efforts were made
to improve external audit, under the PSRMAC., including clarification of responsibilities among the
various audit institutions such as the Chamber of Accounts (CA) - the equivalent of a Supreme Audit
Institution, the State Financial Inspectorate (SF:) under the Primne Minister's office and Internal Audit
Department in the Ministry of Finance. The Law on the Chamber of' Accounts6, established a 13-member
CA, reporting to both to the President and the Parliament. In 2000, the SFI was merged with the
Chamber of Accounts with a view to avoid duplication of funclions.
39.    The external audit function, however, continues to have some deficiencies, as detailed in a recent
Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CZFAA). The indepenidence of the CA is compromised by
the fact that President approves its structure, staffing and emxrployee salaries and Ministry of Finance
approves its budget. Besides the Republican budget, the CA is also entitled to receive 10 percent of the
amount recovered to the budget as a result of its activities. This financing arrangement leads to perverse
incentives. Further, while the Law requires the CA to 'control' a large number of financial transactions, it
does not require it to prepare a comprehensive re port on the audit of the annual financial Statements of the
Government. There are also no requirements regarding the adoption of audit standards. The majority of
the staff in the Chamber of Accounts was transf5,rred from the SFI. The staff, though proficient in
conducting compliance audits, lack skills in and exposure to financial audits.
40.    The NPRS includes the Government's objective of improving external audit to strengthen
accountability and transparency in Government spending. 'Witlh this end in view, before release of the
second tranche, amendments to the Law on the Chamber of Accounts, satisfactory to IDA,would be
enacted to inter alia, (i) ensure that the Budget Committee of the Parliament has an opportunity to review,
jointly with the Ministry of Finance, the draft bLidget of the Chamber of Accounts prior to its submission
to the Government; (ii) eliminate the provision entitling the Chamber of Accounts to retain 10 percent of
the amounts recovered to the budget as a result of its activity, aund provide compensatory funding through
the State budget; and (iii) adopt international audit standards acceptable to the Association. From the
Fiscal year 2005 onwards, the Budget Committee of the Parliament will review, jointly with the Minmstry
of Finance, the draft budget of the Chamber of Accounts prior to its submission to the Government.
Technical assistance to strengthen the capacity of the CA will be financed by DFID.
B. INCREASE EFFICIENCY, EFFECTIVENESS AND) ACCOUNTABILITY WITHIN THE
PUBLIC SECTOR
(i)    Strengthening Public Expenditure Man,agement
41.    A transparent, well-performing public finance system is essential for good governance and
effective economic management. It ensures thai: macro-economic discipline is maintained; scarce public
resources are allocated according to the Govermlent's priorities; funcls are spent according to allocations
approved by Parliament; transactions are properly documented; and regular reports on the budget are
6 Law on the Chamber of Accounts of the Kyrgyz Republic. 1998



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003      13
made available to the Parliament and public, to enable them to hold the Government accountable for
public expenditures and performance.
42.     Since independence, the Kyrgyz Republic has made important progress in establishing the basic
elements of public expenditure management (PEM) institutions, including a legal framework and
procedures for budget management, a basic framework for inter-governmental finance relation, a
Treasury system -the first among CIS, and an external audit function. These reforms, however, are only a
first step in a long and continuous process. Several issues remain to be addressed to ensure the
sustainability and effective functioning of the PEM system. These include: (a) ineffective and
unpredictable budget allocations as a result of weak budget policy formulation processes, (b) inadequate
implementation of the inter-governmental finance system, (c) limited functionality of a manual Treasury
system and weaknesses in budget execution and (d) weak financial and managerial accountability as a
result of underdeveloped internal control systems and inadequate quality of External Audits. The
Government's reform agenda on public financial management included in the NPRS encompasses all
these issues. The main challenge is to deepen the first generation reforms in order to respond to new
realities of handling a significant fiscal adjustment, allocating resources to poverty reduction programs
and addressing the consequences of weak governance and public administration.
(a)     Establishing a more Strategic and Transparent Budget Formulation Process
43.     The main objective of the reforms in this area will be to remove the structural impediments to a
more strategic and open budget policy formulation. Reforms will aim to improve the linkages between
policy planning and resource allocation and establish the budget as the main strategic instrument for
formulating, implementing and monitoring public programs. By strengthening the policy focus of the
budget, the Government will aim to establish a framework for prioritization of public expenditure
programs, and integration of the objectives of the CDF and NSPR within the budget. A more transparent
and strategic policy formulation process is also likely to create a buffer against pressures from vested
interests, which become difficult to resist in a policy vacuum.
44.     The Government has intensified its efforts towards more strategic budgeting. It is making good
progress with the preparation of a simplified version of a Medium Term Budget Framework (MTBF) for
2004-2006, which aims to combine three important elements in budget formulation, the macroeconomic
framework, sectoral strategies and expenditure plans to implement these strategies. However, a
substantial agenda remains to expand and fully institutionalize the MTBF process as the main instrument
for Government's budget policy formulation. Initial actions include (i) consolidation of the economic
policy decision making process; (ii) improvements in the format for presenting the budget to the
Parliament; and (iii) better integration of the recurrent and investment planning processes.
45.     Integrating the decision making process. Economic policy and budget decision making is
fragmented between the Budget Commission7 which reviews and approves the recurrent budget and the
Goskominvest Committee8 which reviews and approves the Public Investment Program (PIP) and the
Development Budget (annual investment plan). Subsequently, both budgets are consolidated (as
aggregates) and sent to the entire cabinet for approval. This practice leads to incoherent decisions with
regard to macro and fiscal discipline, sector policies and resource allocation, which in turn can undermine
the effectiveness of the MTBF.
7The Budget Commission is established every year at the start of the budget process by decree of the Government and it is chaired by the
Minister of Finance. It includes representatives of the Prime Minister's Office, and several ministers , State commnittees and admninistrative
agencies.. The commission also includes representatives of the Legislative Assembly and People's Representative Assembly.
' Goskominvest committee is a permanent committee of the Government that reviews approves and coordinates foreign financed public
investments and economic assistance. It is chaired by the PM and has the First Vice PM, the Governor of the National Bank, various ministers,
Heads of Regional State administrations, Heads of local self Govemments and the Mayor of Bisbkek as its members.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Aidjusbnent Credit - Pr ograrn Docuomert,, April 21, 2003  14
46.    The Government has recently established an apex level Economic Policy Council (EPC) for
strengthening economic policy rnanagement in the country9. As the Government budget provides the
strongest measure of the Government's participai:ion in the economy, i:he authorities are considering
merging the budget related funci ions of the Goskominvest andi the Budget Commnission into the EPC.
This is to ensure that overall macroeconomic and fiscal policy decisions occur at a point in the policy-
making process that allows the (Government to impose fiscally sound, realistic and binding budget
constraints and make policy trade-offs. By second tranche, the Government will implement appropriate
actions, satisfactory to IDA, for integrating the finctions of the existing Budget Commission and the
budget relatedfunctions of the C;oskominvest in the Economic Poglicy Council.
47.    Integrating recurrent and investment budget pilairuning processes. At present investment and
recurrent budgets are planned separately, to the e 'tent that they are often driven by different objectives
and there is little consideration of recurrent cost implications of investment decisions. Moreover, there is
very little integration between domestic investment planning and foreign investment planning, as the
majority of the investment budget is financed by donors. Budget: classification structures are not
consistent between recurrent and investment budgets. In order to better integrate the budget planning
process, by third tranche, estimal es of recurrent cost implications of alt investment projects will be
included in the Medium Term Budget Framework (MBTF), beginning with the MTBF for 2005-2007.
48.    Enabling the Parliament to review budget policy priorities. The format in which the
Government presents the annual budget to Parliarnent is geared towarcLs the economic expenditure iterms
and agency expenditure levels, rather than sectoral and inter-sectoral policies and priorities. Such forrmat
not only makes it difficult for the Parliament to see what policies the Government is proposing to finance,
but also creates confusion about the authority that can authorize c hanges in the budget. It requires
Parliament to approve even unimportant changes in the course of budget implementation, distracting ils
attention from major policy issues. Before second tranche., the Government will revise the format for
presentation to the Parliament of the annual budget law, with the view to (i) make more transparent the;
policy priorities financed by the budget, and; (ii) present sectoral ceilirgs for two consecutive years,
based on the MTBF; and (iii) integrate recurrent and investment expenditures. By third tranche, the
Government will present to Parliament its annual budget law for 2005, in the revisedformat.
49.    Improving Effectiveness of Inter-Governmental Finanaces. Improvements in Inter-
governmental fiscal relations are directly linked with better budget fonnulation and overall improvements
in revenue generation and expencliture management. The Government has made some progress since
1997, by defining expenditure responsibilities of various Government levels, establishing appropriate
revenue assignments and codifying the framework. for a system of inter govemmental transfers between
the national Govemment and the Oblast level, with support fi-om PSRMAC. The decentralization process
has also progressed with elections of local self Governments (lowest tier of Government) in December
2001.
50.    Implementation of the ref orms, however, has been erratic, especially with regard to shifting of
expenditure responsibilities to sub-regional Governments, calculaLtion and allocation of categorical and
equalization grants and the extension of the rules for revenue sharing al: the lower levels of Government.
Back sliding in several fronts has produced a sericusly distorted structure in which elements of the inter-
govemmental finance system are implemented in a manner, individually and as a whole, that fails to
produce desired outcomes. The implicit overridinig objective of thle cuirent implementation appears to be
EPC is headed by the PM and includes key members ol the Goverrmnent who also participate in Lhe other two committees. It can also call in
other tmembers of the Govemment or other institutions, ss necessary. Finally, the EPC' has the right to consult the Parliament on most matters of
importance.



Kyrgyz Republi: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  15
to provide flexibility to and insulate the Central Government from budgetary risks. This is being done by
shifting those risks to regional and sub-regional Govermments, which are the levels less able to
accommodate such risks.
51.    Making the existing rules transparent: One the significant problems faced in the
implementation of the inter-governmental finance system is the non-transparent application of the existing
rules and procedures for allocating resources to the regional and especially sub-regional Governments.
This has created room for negotiated, unstable, discretionary and unpredictable budgets at the local level.
Local Governments, especially below the Oblast level, are not aware of how categorical and equalization
grants are allocated to them from the higher levels. In addition, various expenditure responsibilities are
shifted to them during budget implementation, especially in cases where they are able to collect more
revenues than planned. Such practices allow room for inefficiencies, discretion and rent seeking in the
course of allocating and executing the budget. To mitigate this problem, the Government has agreed to
make transparent all the existing rules and procedures, as well as the methodology used by the Ministry of
Finance to allocate resources to the local Governments. Before third tranche, the new rules and
procedures for the calculation of all transfers between different levels of government will be developed,
published, and made available to all interested parties, especially local Governments at all levels.
52.    Establishing stable tax sharing at the sub-regional level: The Law on the Basic Principles of
the Budget (1997) defines a tax sharing system between the Central Government and the Oblast
Governments. However, below the Oblast level, tax sharing is very unsystematic and usually subject to
negotiations of rayons and cities with. Such a system does not assure any stability or predictability for the
sub-regional Governments. In addition, these negotiations and the high level of discretion available to the
Oblast to allocate resources to the sub-regional levels, totally undermine the role of elected local self
Govemments. Recently, the government has extended the tax sharing system to the rayon level in
Bishkek City and Jalal-Abad. The Government has also extended the tax sharing system to the rayon
level in three additional oblasts.
(b)    Strengthening Accountability and Transparency in Budget Execution
53.    The main objective of the Government's reform program on budget execution will be to reduce
discretion and non-transparent practices in the disbursement of the budget funds, by improving financial
planning and cash management, bringing all the accounts of the budget institutions under the Treasury
control and phasing out non-cash settlements. It is expected that this will not only make budget execution
more transparent, but it will also improve predictability in the financing of government policies and
programs.
54.    Improving financial planning and cash management. The introduction of a manual Treasury
system in 1996 substantially improved budget execution. However, several weaknesses have emerged,
which undermine the Govemment's capacities to implement policies and programs and create
possibilities for extracting rents and favors during budget execution. The disbursement of budget funds
(spending authorization process) has resulted in an ad hoc system of daily negotiations between the
budget department, Treasury and spending agencies to finance mainly protected expenditure itemsl. This
comes from the lack of a proper system for financial planning and cash management, which is essential in
a country with very limited financial resources and an uneven flow of liquidity in the Govemment
accounts during the year. The existence of special means accounts and other accounts outside the
Treasury General Ledger (TGL) also inhibits good financial planning and cash management during
budget implementation.
'° Protected expenditure items consist mainly of wages, pensions, and social security contributions.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance StruduralAdjustnent Credit- Program Documen4, Aprl 21, 2003  1 6
55.    The Government recognizes these issues and is taking rmeasures to address them. It has already
created a new Financial Planning department willtin the Treasury and is working on streamlining the
MOF procedures for authorizing spending. The Government has also adopted a regulation that
establishes a new procedure for the preparation oF the monthly financial plan. The new procedures for
financial planning will be implemented before release of the second tranche.
56.    Phasing out extra budgetary operations of Budget Institutions. Lack of appropriate cash
management systems in the MOF and the tight controls imposed on the budget institutions in the use of
cash resources have encouraged the emergence o03 fiscal operations oul side the Government budget, in
violation of the Law on the KR Treasury1. Over 40 percent cf the aggregate extra budgetary resources at
the end of 200012 were held outside the Treasury Single Account (TSA), probably in cash or other bank
accounts. Almost every central Budget Institution maintains a so called "Special Means Accounts",
which, until recently, was operating outside the TSA. The amount of inoney deposited in these accounts
in some cases is equal to a Budget Institution's regular budget allocation.
57.    The Government has initiated various actions to control the opening of such accounts, including
the reiteration of the basic provisions in the Annual Budget L:aw for 2002. However, a more systematic
approach needs to be adopted with regard to controlling extra budgetary fiscal operations of any form. By
second tranche, the Government will adopt an Action Plan, satisjfactory to IDA, for transferring all
Extra-budgetary Accounts, with the exception of the Social Flund, into the Treasury General Ledger and
make satisfactory progress in its implementation. By third trannc he, the transfer of all Extra-budgetary
Accounts, with the exception of the Social Fund, into the Treasury General Ledger will be completed..
58.    Phasing out off-set transactions in the Treasury. There is a widespread use of off-set
transactions within the Treasury for paying utilities and some contractors. Such transactions have reached
a magnitude of around 25-30 percent of the annual budget (practically all the utility payments). These off-
sets are legitimized in the Treasury Manual, which permits the settlement of dues between the
Government and the suppliers of goods and services on a non-cash barter basis. While the Government
has designed special accounting rules to record these transaclions, they present inappropriate budget
management practices and might conceal liquidity problems in the real sectors, such as energy, for paying
tax liabilities to the Government's budget. Moreover they undermine linancial planning since the
amounts dedicated to off-sets become by default non-discretionary spending and cannot be reallocated.
The Government will issue a resolution, adopting an Action Pl] an for phasing out off-sets and non-cash
transactions in the budget and the Social Fund, satisfactory to IDA, by second tranche. By third tranche,
the Government will make satisfactory progress in the implementation of the Action Plan.
59.    Establishing systematic reconciliation between the Treasury records on payments made mnd
reimbursements received from the agent banks. At present there is no reconciliation between the
Treasury records on payments made during the day on behalf of the Government and the amount of
reimbursement to the agent banks for these transactions. This process creates possibilities for the agent
banks to over-report on payments, be over-reimbursed from the National Bank of KR (NBKR) and utilize
the float during the month for their own purposes. Apart frorn the ineflfective management of the
Government's idle funds, such practice may create possibilities for non -transparent use of the funds on
the bank's side as well as conceal difficulties they might have in mneetinig reserve requirements. A
comparison between the Treasury records and the Settlements Saving Company (SSC) (one of the main
agent banks) for the month of December 2001 revealed major differences. At the end of December 2001
the cumulative difference was around 5 million soms"3. To address these problems, the MOF has
L Law on the Principles of the Treasury of the Kyrgyz Republic passed on May 28w'. 1994

recently implemented a systematic mechanism for daily reconciliation of Treasury transactions and
reimbursements from the NBKR.
60.    The improvements in budget execution mentioned above will be complemented by the
modemization of the Treasury with assistance from the GTAC.
(ii)  Improving the Internal Control Environment
61.    While an external audit function is important to provide Parliament and outside stakeholders a
means to hold the executive responsible, within public entities, there is a need for a strong internal audit
function to assess financial and operational risks, design appropriate intemal control systems to mitigate
the risks and conduct internal audits to assure management that the control systems are working and the
organization is operating in accordance with applicable laws and regulations.
62.    The Intemal Audit function in the Kyrgyz Republic is underdeveloped. The Ministry of Finance
continues to maintain a small internal audit unit to carry out compliance tests for treasury transactions.
However, currently, there are no internal audit units within the line ministries, except in the Ministry of
Labor and Social Protection. There is a lack of clear legal obligations requiring line ministries to
maintain an adequate intemal control framework. Ad hoc steps taken to ensure financial probity have
proved to be ineffective in preventing abuse and waste of resources, as is indicated by several instances of
non-compliance with laws and fraud/corruption reported by the CA in its annual reports.
63.    The Govemment recognizes the need to strengthen the internal control environment within the
public sector. Therefore, by second tranche, the Government will develop a Strategy for the creation of
an effective intemal audit function in public entities. By third tranche release, it will issue an appropriate
legal instrument, satisfactory to IDA, to implement the Strategy. The Government will also implement
the Strategy in at least three Ministries, by the third tranche.
(iii)  Improving Transparency, Value for Money and Accountability in Public Procurement
64.    Procurement of goods, services and works constitutes about 35 percent of budgetary
expenditures. An open, competitive procurement process, conducted on a level playing field, has the
potential of significantly increasing the efficiency of this substantial amount of public spending. The
resulting savings would have the implicit effect of expanding the envelope of scarce resources, thus,
enhancing the ability of the State to better implement public policies. At the same time, it is important to
increase the transparency and accountability in procurement, as this is an area where the risk of corruption
is very high.
65.   In 1997, a Public Procurement Law (PPL) based on the UNCITRAL Model Law was enacted.
This law established a decentralized procurement system, under which procurement is carried out by
individual public entities; created an independent oversight body (the State Procurement Agency), with
authority to issue regulations; and established modern procurement processes. However, as indicated by
the Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR), conducted in 2002, the procurement system
continues to suffer from various organizational, normative, procedural, fnancial and capacity
deficiencies.
66.    The State Procurement Agency was merged with the State Material Reserves Fund in 2001, to
form the State Commission on Public Procurement and Material Reserves (SCPPMR). This has created a
potential conflict of interest, as the agency is now responsible for both procuring for the Reserves and for
supervising itself in connection with those procurements. The organizational structure of the SCPPMR
needs to be improved to enhance its capacity in legal, monitoring and evaluation, and procurement



Kyrgyz RepubUic: Governance Structara& Adjustment Credit - .r,'grwn Document,, April 21, 2003  18
capacity building functions. The funding mechanisms of thc SCPPM/[R also needs to be changed. While
part of the costs of the agency are met by budgetary allocations, it is also entitled to receive 1 percent of
notional savings made on conthacts concluded through unlimited tendlers, creating conflict of interest. By
second tranche, the Governrnent will prepare and implement a restructuring plan, satisfactory to IDA, for
the State Commission on Public Procurement and Material Reserves (SCPPMR), to separate the
procurement function from the functions of the Slate Material Reserves, within the SCPPMR; strengthen
the legal, monitoring and evaluation and procu rement capacity building functions of the SPA; and
strengthen the capacity of procuring entities.
67.    There has been a proliferation of procurement rules issued by different authorities. Since 1998,
26 decrees, instructions and orders, dealing with differerii: aspects of procurement, have been issued.
Many of these rules have led to confusion in the division of responsibilities and distortions in
procurement practices. There also appear to be Frequent deviations from procurement procedures
established by the PPL, for instance, procuring entities frequently disclose estimated contract values to
bidders, extend bid submission deadlines inforrnally and sometimes negotiate contact prices with selected
bidders. Although the PPL does not stipulate it, the SCPP'NIMR has assumed a direct role in procurement
through review of bidding documents, evaluatiorn reports, contracts, etc. This makes the procuring
entities excessively dependent on the SCPPMR mnd seriously comprcmises the latter's oversight and
supervision responsibilities.
68.    The efficiency and cost effectiveness of public prOcureenent is also affected by deficiencies in
budget execution. Public agencies enter into contractual commitmenms based on their approved budget
allocations. But due to cash flow constraints of Lhe Treasury, they do not receive the required funds in a
timely manner. Therefore, payments to contractors are frequently deiayed. This discourages enterprises
from bidding for Government contracts, thus, lirriting competition. It also raises the cost of goods and
services procured, as contractors factor the cost of potential )ayment delays in their bid prices. There is
no training strategy or program for providing procurement training to public servants on a regular basis.
As a result, procurement is being conducted largely by untrained and unqualified public officials.
69.   In order to deal with the aforesaid deficiencies, the Government has already confirmed its
agreement with the Country Procurement Assessment Review (CPAR) Action Plan. By second tranche,
the Government will implement the activities pertaining to 2003 included in the CPAR Action Plan. By
third tranche, the implementation of the remaining activities of the CBAR Action Plan will be completed.
In addition, the Government will prepare and irrrplement a Procurement Training Strategy and introduce
an attestation program for Procurement Officers, to raise the qualification level of persons engaged in
procurement.
70.    In order to enhance accountability and transparency in the pu blic procurement, before second and
third tranche, the Government will arrange to cairy out procturement audits of selected, high value items,
to be agreed with IDA, that affect service quality in the Health and Ecducation sectors (e.g. selected
pharmaceuticals on the List of Essential Drugs, text books etc.). The audits will verify compliance with
the procurement rules and regulations, assess value for money with reference to international and national
market prices and recommend consequential renmedial actions. The procurement audit reports will be
made public.
(iv)   Improving Service Delivery in the Health, Education and Social Protection Sectors
71.    The health sector of the Kyrgyz Republic, similar to those of other transition economies, is facing
the challenge of providing quality health services to the popu]lation in the context of shrinking public
expenditures on health care, an excessive number of health facil ities and personnel and a weak emphasis



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003              19
on cost-effective preventive and primary health care approaches. To address these problems, since 1996,
the Kyrgyz Republic has been implementing a comprehensive health sector reform prograrn, in a phased
nanner14. Key elements of the reform program include: (i) rationalizing health facilities and personnel;
(ii) improving equity in the geographical allocation of resources, (iii) making the health system more cost-
effcctivfe by strengthening preventive and primary health care services, (iv) making providers accountable
f-or efficienlcy, quality and access at the health facility level, and (iv) reducing corruption in the health
sector. New health care financing (the single payer reform'5), was introduced in two regions in 2001 and
in two more regions in 2002. It extends the output-based purchasing methods of the Mandatory Health
Fisuirance Fund (MHBIF) to budget funds and also includes the formalization of hospital co-payments.
16
Implementation progress on health reforms is satisfactory, but critical challenges remain . In particular,
the organizational changes in primary care have not been supported by a corresponding shift in resource
allocation from the tertiary to the primary level.
72.     Single Payer Law: Implementation of recent reforms in the health sector and especially the
introdu-ction of the Single Payer System requires changes in institutional arrangements for budget
r-a agement. As noted above, these changes relate to the output-based purchasing and pooling of health
rec,sources, This new practice is quite different from the current process of input based budgeting, where
I.ea]th providers (e.g. hospitals) are financed on the basis of physical norms (number of beds, staff).
A*lthoiigb some changes in budget management practices for the health sector have taken place in selected
Oblasts, these are based on temporary regulations rather than legislation, and this puts the health sector
reforrn process in a rather tenuous position. Another problem, especially in those oblasts that are
iinplernenting the formal co-payment policy for hospitalizations, is unplanned and non-transparent re-
allocations from health to other sectors. Therefore, suitable changes need to be made in the legislative
basis for budget management and other reforms in the health sector, while ensuring that these are
consistennt with overall budget management and financial accountability systems. By second tranche, the
Govenrment wvill enact a Single-payer Law, satisfactory to IDA.
73.     Establishing the Bishkek Territorial Health Insurance Fund: Despite the progress in the
health sector, there have been few changes in the allocation of public funds towards primary care and
regions with the greatest health needs. For example, hospitals consume 70 percent of public expenditures,
and the geographical allocation of resources is skewed with Bishkek being favored over the other regions.
Tmprovements in allocative efficiency and geographical equity of public funds are needed to improve the
poverty impact of public expenditures on health care. Reallocation is contingent on the implementation
of the single-payer system in all oblasts and, especially, in Bishkek .  Before second tranche, the
Govemment will issue a decree, satisfactory to IDA, establishing the Bishkek Territorial Health Insurance
Fund and the rules and regulations guiding the operation of the Fund.
74.     Financing of Healthcare Insurance for Pensioners and the Unemployed: Since its
establishment in 1997, the MiHIF has faced the challenge of timely and adequate transfer of funds from
the Social Fund (SF) for employed persons and pensioners and from the Republican budget for children
and persons with social benefits"8. Among all categories, transfers for pensioners is the most problematic.
For example, between 1997-2002, only 18% of planned revenues for pensioners has been transferred to
The Manas Health Reform Program.
" The siugle payer system refers to an arrangement where all funds at the oblast level (from oblast, rayon and city) are collected within a single
crganization (TDMHIP). The purpose of the single payer reform is to reduce the fragmentation and duplication among the various levels (oblast,
rayon and eity) and streamline the financing and delivery of health services. Since one payer is responsible for contracting of health services, this
Trd,uees zidrinistrative costs and also strengthens the abiity of the MHI] to offer a coherent set of incentives to providers.
