the geographical equity and increase spending on public health, preventive and primary health care services.

Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Docurnent,, April 21, 2003  20
the MHIF. Pensioners are generally heavy users of heallh services, and the fact that the MHIF does not
receive adequate funding for th is is one of conlibuting factors for MIHIF financial stress. By second
tranche, the Govemment will amiend current legislation to establish iesponsibility of the Republican
budget for the financing of allocations for healthcare insurance of pensioners and unemployed. The 2005
budget will include funds for tIis expenditure ilem.
75.    Rationalizing Finctioinal and Expendtiture Responsibilities in Health and Education
Sectors: In order to advance reforms in health and education, there is a need to have a fresh look at how
expenditure responsibilities can be streamlined and made stable among various Govemment levels to
ensure better service delivery. The Government will, by second tranche, prepare draft amendments to the
relevant legislation, satisfactory to IDA, to streamline and clearly de ine functional and expenditure
responsibilities in the health sector. By the thini tranche, the G overrnment will amend legislation,
satisfactory to IDA, to streamline and clearly define functional and expenditure responsibilities in the
health sector. The Government will also amend legislation, satisfactry to IDA, to streamline and clearly
define functional and expenditure responsibilities in the education sector, by third tranche.
76.    Establishing a system for defining minimum service standlards in Health and Educatioii
Sectors: In order to assure local fulfillment of r egional and national priorities, minimum service
requirerments must be defined for priority services. Without this, there is no basis for determining
appropriate financing, or holding sub-national CGovernments accountable for the proper execution of
expenditure responsibilities. To the degree thai: standards currently exist in the Kyrgyz Republic, they are
based on physical and facility riorms and are nor: conducive lo assuring any meaningful level of
accomplishment. Especially in the health sector, where a diiffer-ent financing and service provision system
is being implemented, the use of inputs and physical norms as a means for allocating expenditures has
proven outdated. It is important therefore, to start the development cf service standards. Before second
tranche, the Govemment will issue decrees defining the melhodology for developing minimum service
standards in the education sector and refining those in the hIealth sector.
77.    Categorical Grants for Health and Education: An important problem affecting basic service
delivery is local Government contributions to the health sector, especially from categorical grants (CG).
The resource allocation formula under the CG iE linked to inputs (mainly salaries) and therefore
negatively affects health sector allocations which are incre.asingly moving towards output-based
allocations. The design of CGs needs to be synchronized wlith tLhe current developments in the health'
system. In the education sector, the practice of financing teacher saliry costs under the Republican
budget treats existing teacher costs as non-discretionary and hence tends to legitimate what may be an
inefficient delivery of education. A preferred basis for central financing of education costs is through a
differentiated capitation system of financing, which pays for education on the basis of the number of
students enrolled in each type of program. Under this approach, it is important that the financing formula
reflect intrinsic sources of higher costs - such as dispersed -population, and a higher-than-average density
of students with specials learning needs. But developing such a formula will require preparation in the
form of a careful examination of the sources and magnitudes of intrinsic cost differences prior to
implementation of the formula. Another problem is that the curTent financing formula provides virtually
no financing for in-service teacher training. Fai]ure to fund teacher t-aining is a high-risk and low-
efficiency option, because it means that the education systemn's most important asset - its teachers - are
not equipped to perform their work effectively, especially in introducing the important innovations
planned under the education reform. As a condition of second tranche release, the Government will
adopt an appropriate legal act ro define capitation-basedfoirmula for calculating categorical grants in
the health and education sector The new formnuae will be irnplemented before the third tranche release.



Kyrgyz Republc: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, Aprd 21, 2003         21
78.     Costs of Pre-University Education: In principle, local Governments finance primary and
secondary schools from their own resources. The current practice of financing teachers' salaries under
the Republican budget has the very compelling advantage that it ensures that even the poorest localities
are able to pay teachers' salaries and keep their schools open. But it has a serious disadvantage in that it
does not include financing for textbooks. Textbooks are financed by parents under a textbook rental
scheme. Although rental charges appear modest in an absolute sense, the extremely low level of incomes
means that many households cannot afford the cost of textbook rental, thus preventing children from
having textbooks. This is a major reason for low quality and incomplete school attendance in poor areas
of the country - including many rural areas. By second tranche, the Government will develop and
implement a plan to modify the textbook rental scheme to ensure that low household income does not
prevent students from receivng textbooks.
79.     Social Protection: Pension reform in the Kyrgyz Republic is at a critical juncture. The recently
completed Public Expenditure Review highlights the need for the Government to clarify priorities among
the multiple objectives of the current pension system, based on the 1997 reforms. Proposals for a new,
fully funded pillar within the Kyrgyz pension system have been advocated because of a concern that
incentives for contributors to participate in the current system are inadequate, and a belief that
introduction of a funded pillar could promote economic growth by raising the level of savings and
encouraging the development of financial and capital markets. The Bank's technical assessment of the
proposal for a fully-funded pillar indicates the need for first investigating the prerequisite costs, and
feasibility of this approach in current Kyrgyz circumstances, before taking any irrevocable steps to create
and implement a funded system. By second tranche, the Government will identify the prerequisites and
necessary lead-time for adopting a funded approach to pensions by developing an action program and
timetable, satisfactory to IDA, for creating the policies, institutions and systems required to introduce a
fully funded element in to the basic structure of the Kyrgyz pensions system. By third tranche, the
Government will demonstrate progress, satisfactory to IDA, in implementing an agreed subset of the
action plan required to introduce a fully funded element into the basic structure of the Kyrgyz pensions
system.
(v)     Enhancing the Effectiveness of the Civil Service
80.     The country is in the initial stages of developing a professional civil service'9. A Law on Civil
Service (LCS), enacted in 1999, defines the civil service in very broad terms and covers political
appointees, such as the Prime Minister, members of the Government, nominees of the President and
Deputies of the Parliament and their assistants and advisers, as well as career civil servants. Thus, the
line between permanent civil servants and the political executive is blurred. Political appointments also
extend quite deep down the administrative hierarchy. Changes in political heads of ministries and
agencies, therefore, lead to large scale changes in incumbents at lower levels. The LCS also provides
inadequate protection to career civil servants against politically motivated dismissals. As a result, the
independence, impartiality and quality of advice provided by them to the political executive suffers.
81.     While the LCS and associated regulations have created a basic framework for human resource
management in the civil service, this framework has not been fully institutionalized. In addition, the
current rules need to be improved in several respects. Remuneration of the civil service, as also of other
public services, remains way below competitive levels. Low salaries lead to low quality of public
services, encourage misuse of regulatory authority and create incentives for corruption. Turnover of staff
at skilled levels is quite high. Although the Government has carried out retrenchment of staff in the past
"9IThe terms 'Civil Service' or 'Civil Servant' are meant to include permanent public employees, exercising State authority as managers and staff
in Ministries, State Agencies, State Committees, State Comrnissions, Oblasts, Rayons and Local Governments, as distinguished from political
appointees, support staff, teachers, healthcare workers, uniformed services and workers in State Owned Enterprises. The terms 'Public Service'
or 'Pubic Servant' are used in a broader sense and refer to all persons paid out of the national budget.



Kyrgyz RepubiUc: Governance StructurGaI Adjustment Credit - Program DocNwwent, April21, 2003  22
years, there still appears to be scope for further nghtsizing. While new skills are sorely needed to equip
civil servants to handle the evolving role of the State, no systematic training needs assessment is carried
out. Most training is ad hoc and depends on donor funding. Finally, the lack of adequate institutional
arrangements for managing the civil service is i major deficiency. The capacity of the Civil Sector
Division in the President's Administration, which performs this function, is extremely lirnitedl, as is the
capacity of the personnel departments of mninisxies and agencies.
82.    The Government is keenly aware of the need for a well-qual]ified, professional civil service, that
would assist it in formulating ;md implementing policies f0r effective governance and poverty reduction.
It has taken various measures tn the recent past towards this objective. Merit-based competitive
recruitment was launched in 2001; the compressi on ratio cf sa'laries in the CS was increased to improve
motivation; a process of assigning Class Ranks to civil servants was initiated in 2001; in order to trim the
size of the civil service 1,200 civil servants were retrenched in 2001 and 1750 civil servants and managers
in budgetary entities are expected to be retrenched in 2002; a Presidential decree on Ethics of Civil
Servants was issued and Ethics Comnnissions were createcl at the level of the President's Administration
and other Ministries and Central agencies. In continuation of its efforts, the Government has initiaced
discussion of draft amendments to the Law on Civil Service with relevant stakeholders. By second
tranche, the Government will: (i) enact amendmnents to the Law on Civil Service, satisfactory to IDA, with
a view to separate political appointees and co reer civil servants; increase competitiveness irn recrui'tment
and promotion; reduce politicail interference in personnel management decisions; improve incentives for
performance and integrity; and improve institutional arrangements for management of the civil service.
By third tranche, the Government will: (i) establish the Civil Service Management Agency (CSMA) and
ensure that it is fully operational, by appointing management and staff, providing adequate budgetary
resources, and developing rules and regulations for the functioning of the agency. In addition, before
third tranche, the Government will take measuies to ensure thalt all civil servants recruited for the fi st
time to 'main' and 'senior' positions are recruiled on the basis of open competition and rnerit, in
accordance with the Civil Service Law and that all persons promoted to "main" and "senior" positions are
promoted on the basis of results of performance evaluation or attestation. Further, the Government will
implement a revised remuneration structure for the civil service, satisfactory to IDA, before the third
tranche.
(vi)  Streamlining Structure of Government Ministries and Agencies
83.    The State in the Kyrgyz Republic, like all other FS1J countries, inherited a wide range of
functions. While its role has shrunk since then., its span of activities continues to be too large relative to
its resources and capacity. The roles and responsibilities of Governmnent at the central, oblast, rayon aLnd
local level are not entirely clear. At the central Government level, there is duplication and overlapping of
functions between different ministries and between different units, cepartments and agencies under
individual ministries. At the regional level, there is ambiguity about the authority of the governors and
the ministries over subordinate organizational tnits and their accountability for service delivery.
84.    In addition to the functional review of the Ministry of the Health mentioned above, the
Government has carried out functional reviews of the organizational structure of the Ministries of
Education and Agriculture and Water Resources, with a view to rationalize the organization structure and
remove duplication of functions. Other ministries and agencies are in the process of carrying out thzir
own functional reviews, with assistance from DFID and UND]?. By second tranche, the Government will
extend Functional Reviews of Ministries of Health, Education and Labor and Social Development and
one additional ministry to be agreed with IDA, to cover their local branches and affiliated agencies, such
as the Mandatory Health Insurance Fund. It will also prepare an action plan for rightsizing of staff in
these Ministries and agencies, based on the results of the Ifunctional reviews. By third tranche, the
Govemment will make satisfactory progress in. implementation of recommendations of the functional



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, Apil 21, 2003  23
reviews in the Ministries of Health, Education andLabor and Social Development and one additional
ministry to be agreed with IDA,, at the central and local levels, and in their affiliated agencies. It will also
make satisfactory progress in implementing the action plan for rightsizing of staff in these ministries and
agencies.
IV. THE PROPOSED GOVERNANCE STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT CREDIT
85.    The proposed Governance Structural Adjustment Credit (GSAC), would support the
Government's program discussed above. Focusing primarily on the executive branch of the State, the
operation would have two complimentary objectives: (a) to improve the transparency and responsiveness
of the public sector and enhance the ability of external stakeholders to hold it accountable; and (b) to
increase efficiency, effectiveness and accountability within the public sector. With regard to the first
objective, the GSAC would support reforms aimed at (i) improving personal accountability of political
officials and civil servants; (ii) strengthening voice and participation by improving public access to
official information; (iii) improving the interaction between regulatory agencies and business enterprises;
and (iv) strengthening external audit. To advance the second objective, the operation would support
reforms directed at (i) strengthening public expenditure management, by establishing a more strategic and
transparent budget formulation process, and strengthening accountability and transparency in budget
execution; (ii) improving the internal control environment; (iii) improving transparency, value for money
and accountability in public procurement; (iv) improving service delivery in the health and education
sectors; (v) enhancing the effectiveness of the civil service; and (vi) streamlining the structure of
Government ministries and agencies.
86.    Borrower and Credit Amount: The Borrower is the Kyrgyz Republic. The Credit amount would
be SDR 14.7 million (US$ 20 million equivalent). The Credit would be on standard IDA terms, with a 40
year maturity, including a 10-year grace period.
87.    Relation to Country Assistance Strategy: In line with the NPRS, the Country Assistance
Strategy, Report # 25708-KG dated April 22, 2003, identifies three key priorities: (a) supporting
sustainable private sector-led growth, (b) providing essential services and (c) strengthening the
Governance Framework20. The proposed Governance Structural Adjustment Credit (GSAC) would focus
primarily on the third goal. It would, however, also contribute to the attainment of the first two goals. To
improve governance, the operation would, on the one hand, support measures to increase the
transparency, openness and accountability of the public sector. On the other hand, it would aim to
strengthen the institutional framework and capacity of the public sector in key operational areas, so as to
increase its effectiveness and efficiency. To facilitate private sector-led growth, it would help the
government rationalize some of its important regulatory activities that impose significant costs on finms.
Finally, to improve the quality of essential services, the GSAC would support reforms in the health and
education sectors.
88.    Rationalefor Bank Involvement: The reforms included in the Government's program are
urgently needed to successfully implement the poverty reduction agenda. The Bank has been actively
involved in reforming key public sector institutions through various operations, including the Public
Sector Resource Management Adjustment Credit (PSRMAC), Institutional Development Fund (IDF)
Grants for development of public procurement, and projects to reform the Health sector. Recent
economic and sector work, especially, the Public Expenditure Review (PER), the Country Financial
Accountability Assessment (CFAA), Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) have also focused
' The Country Assistance Strategy( Report No. 17641-KG, April 14, 1998) and CAS Progress Report (Report No. 23185-KG, November 9,
2001), supported four major strategic objectives of the Kyrgyz Republic: attaining sustainable growth, reducing poverty, improving governance
and strengthening public finances. These goals are also consistent with those of the GSAC.



Kyrgyz Repubblc: Governance StructuralAdjushnent Credit-- Program Docisntent,, April 21, 2003                   24
on identifying critical weaknesses in public sector management. In the meantime, the Government has
undertaken additional reforms on its own, or v ith the assistance of other donors.
89.      The objective of the GSAC is to maintain the momentlum olF the reform process and provide a
coherent framework in which it could be carried forward. The operation would help deepen the reforms
that have already been implemented, assist in removing distortions that have crept into some areas over
time, initiate reforms in a few additional areas and prov ide leverage for implementation of ongoing
reforms that need support at the top levels of the Government. In developing the GSAC agenda,
extensive consultations have been held with the Government and the donor community. As a result, there
is considerable consensus around the program       and donors have expressed their willingness to participate
in the program through Grant assistance. Thus, the GSAC wilI be a vehicle for the Government to
mobilize grant resources for its public sector reform program. Given the Bank's experience in
implementing similar reforms in the Kyrgyz Republic and in other countries, its country knowledge and
strong relationships with counterparts and stakeholders, it wotuld have a significant positive impact on the
quality, pace and realism of the reforms. The 13ank will a lso play a key role in coordinating assistance
provided by different donors.
90.      Monitorable Actions and Tranche Release Conditions: Tranche release will be dependent on (i)
an acceptable macro-economic policy framework being in place; (ii) the satisfactory implementation of
the Government's Program described in the Letter of Development Policy (Annex 3) and (iii) the
fulfillment of the specific tranche release actions (Table 2, below). All Board presentation conditions have
been met.

91.       Tranching: The Credit would be released in three tranches. The first tranche of SDR 3.675
million (US$ 5 million equivalent) will be released upon Effectiveness, the second tranche of SDR 3.675
million (US$ 5 million equivalent) is expected to be released in first quarter/ second of 2004 on
fulfillment of second tranche release conditions, and the third tranche of SDR 7.350 million (US$ 10
million equivalent) is expected to be released in the first/second quarter of 2005 on fulfillment of third
tranche release conditions.

93.     Environmental Category: The operation will have iio direct impact on the environment. As such
it is rated 'U' and does not require an environmental assessment.
94.    Disbursement and Auditing: Disbursements under the proposed credit will be made to art
account ("Deposit Accounts") of the Ministry of Finance establish,-d at the National Bank of the Kyrgyz
Republic. The credit will be disbursed accorcling to the ]3,ank's simplified disbursement procedures iFor
adjustment operations. Thus disbursements will not be linked to specific purchases and there will be no
procurement requirements. If the credit proceeds are used for ineliigible purposes, the International
Development Association (IDA) will require the Borrower to either (a) return that amount to the account
for use for eligible purposes; or (b) refund the amount directly to IDA, in which case IDA will cancel an
equivalent undisbursed amount of the credit. IDA will require an audit of the deposit account. The audit
reports would be submitted to IDA no more than six months after the date of IDA's request. The expected
closing date of the proposed Credit is June 30, 2005.
95.     The Ministry of Finance and the National Bank of the Kyrgyz Republic have in place the basic
financial management systems in terms of accounting, reporting, auditing and internal controls. The
NBKR adopted IAS in 1997 and is audited by internationailly recognized auditing firms. As the IMF
intensifies its safeguards assessments, there will be consultations with the IMiF and disbursements under
the credit will be dependent upon a continuing favorable assessment by the IMF.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  31
96.    To date, the country strategy and program has supported the improvement in financial
management capacity, most notably through the Public Sector Resource Management Adjustment Credit
(PSRMAC), the Financial Sector Adjustment Credit (FINSAC) and under the proposed operations i.e.
GSAC and GTAC. Budget execution was improved in mid-1998 when a system of warrants was
introduced that required Treasury authorization of payments on the basis of commitments and the budget
framework was improved through an IDA supported Law on the Principles of Budget.
97.    Management, Reporting and Supervision: The program will be implemented by a Coordination
Council (CC). It is chaired by the Deputy Head of the President's Administration and the Vice Prime
Minister is the Deputy Chairman. The Minister of Finance, as well as other officials of the Government,
will be members of the Coordination Council. A Project Management Group (PMG), reporting to the
Council will be established to facilitate and coordinate implementation of the reform program, including
the management of technical assistance. The PMG will submit progress reports to the Government and
the IDA, one month after the end of each quarter. Technical Committees, whose members would be
drawn from line managers and staff, will be formed to guide and coordinate the technical aspects of the
reforms and ensure buy-in at the operational level. These Committees would be supported by full-time
project coordinators. Implementation of the program will be supervised through at least two IDA
supervision missions every year. Follow up actions between missions will be taken by staff based at
headquarters and at the Resident Mission at Bishkek. Strong donor coordination will be maintained
during project implementation. This would take the form of joint review of TORs for important
consultancies with relevant donors; coordination of TA activities to ensure that inter-linked activities are
completed on time; exchange of supervision reports; joint supervision missions, wherever feasible; and
discussions with development partners during each supervision mission.
98.    Poverty Category: The operation does not have direct poverty reduction interventions. However,
improvements in govemance and public sector management supported by the Credit will significantly
improve the capacity of the Govermment to implement the National Poverty Reduction Strategy.
99.    Collaboration with IMF and other donors: The preparation of the GSAC has been closely
coordinated with the IMF. The operation is consistent with and complements the structural reform
measures included in the PRGF. A member of the Task Team also participated in the Fund mission to
review the working of the Kyrgyz Treasury System. Extensive consultations have been held during the
design of the Credit with the DFID, UNDP, EU-TACIS, USAID, ADB, the Swiss Aid Agency and their
consultants, with a view to take into account ongoing initiatives, share knowledge and coordinate and
mobilize grant funds for technical assistance.
100.  Benefits: The GSAC would increase the ability of citizens, civil society and the private sector to
demand improvements in State performance. This would result from enhanced access to information
about key aspects of public sector operations and increased participation in State activities. In parallel,
the program would strengthen core competencies within the public sector. Reforms in public expenditure
management would strengthen the linkage between policies and public spending, better align resource
availability and responsibilities at the lower levels of Govemment and improve efficiency, predictability
and accountability in budget execution. Reforms in public procurement would improve cost-effectiveness
of govemment spending on goods and services. Increased professionalism and capacity of the civil
service would provide much needed skills in critical areas of policy formulation and execution. Finally,
streamlining of the structure of Government would result in improved administrative efficiency and
coordination. On the whole, by simultaneously strengthening the voice of extemal stakeholders and
improving the effectiveness of the Govemment machinery, the operation would enhance the ability of the
State to pursue the objectives of the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS).



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Strutaural Adjustment Credit - Program Docwnment,, Aprl 21, 2003  32
101.   While reducing corruption in the Kyrgyz public sector would require sustained, long-term efforts
on many fronts, the GSAC would contribute 1.o this cause in several ways. Some of the reforms covered
by the operation, such as publication of declarations of incomes and assets, creation of administrative
mechanisms to address grievances of citizens and the private sector and strengthening of disciplinary
rules applicable to civil servants, have a direct anti-coirnption objective. Others would address the
problem indirectly through systemic institutional reforms that increase transparency and accountability.
For instance, increased awareness of State activities and relevant rales and regulations would enable
extemal stakeholders to demand higher standards of conduct and integrity from public officials.
Similarly, enhanced civic participation in the budget process; reduced discretion and informality in budget
execution resulting from modernization of treasury operations; strengthening of internal control and
external audit functions; and increased transparency in tlhe: procurement process would diminish
opportunities for fraud and corruption in public expendiiture management. Finally, the elimination of
financing mechanisms that create perverse incentives for regulatory agencies would reduce the tendency
of these agencies to misuse authority.
102.   Risks: Improvements in public sector perforrmance take a long time to materialize and are
vulnerable to opposition from vested interests. Strong., sustained political commnitment is therefore
absolutely necessary. Waning of political support can significantly slow or even derail the reform.
process. The limited institutional capacity of the GoveniLment also poses a serious risk. Further, the
proposed refonns involve a large number of Government agencies, stakeholders and donors. Deficiencies
in coordination between these actors could also seriously affect implementation. In order to mitigate
these risks, the government's own reform agenda, as spelled out in the NPRS, has been made the core of
the GSAC reforms, so as ensure ownership. The operatioDn has required the Government to undertake
several measures before Board presentation, to demonstrate its coramitment to reform. Also
disbursements have been back-loaded. Significant technical assistance will be provided to augment the
Government's capacity. A broad- based project coordination and management structure has been created
to ensure active involvement of relevant stakeholders and close coordination of different activities.
Notwithstanding these measures, the overall risk of the operation will remain high. This is due to the
inherently difficult nature of institutional and governance reforms, which are subject to a number of,
difficult to control, political, social and economic variables. The high risk, is however, matched by
potentially high rewards.

Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Report of the President, April 21, 2003  46
Annex 3
GOVERNANCE STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT CREDIT
LETTER OF DEVELOPMENT POLICY
1.    The Government of the Kyrgyz Republic has carried out significant reforms in economic policy
since independence. However, reforms in governance and public sector management have not kept pace
with economic policy reformns. Deficiencies in these areas have emerged as major constraints in the
ability of our country to fight poverty. Accordingly, the National Poverty Reduction Strategy that we
have prepared recently gives a high priority to improving the effectiveness of the state. In that strategy
we have outlined several dimensions that would need to be addressed to build an effective state. Those
dimensions include reforms in democratic institutions, law making processes, the judiciary and law
enforcement agencies, public administration, public expenditure management, civil service and local self
Government.
2.     We hope that the proposed Governance Structural Adjustment Credit (GSAC), in coordination
with assistance we are receiving from other donors, will assist us to achieve two complimentary
objectives: (a) enhance the transparency, openness and accountability of the public sector to our external
stakeholders; and (b) improve effectiveness and efficiency within the public sector.
3.     Our Government has taken several initial steps to improve the public sector in the recent past.
These include reforms in public expenditure management under the World Bank financed Public Sector
Resource Management Adjustment Credit (PSRMAC), creation of a Chamber of Accounts, adoption of a
modern Public Procurement Law, enactment of a Civil Service Law, retrenchment of staff at the central
level, reduction in the number of ministries and state bodies, launching of a concerted action plan to
remove administrative barriers to investment, establishment of elected local self Governments,
implementation of major reforms in the health sector, increasing access to information about the
functioning of the state, and efforts to encourage participation of citizens and civil society in public
affairs. We believe that the GSAC operation will assist us to continue and deepen these reforms that have
already been initiated and make incremental improvements in the capability of the state. It would also
help us to prepare the ground for more advanced reforms in the future.
4.     While the experience that our Government has accumulated in implementing the first order
reforms will be extremely helpful, significant technical assistance (TA) would be needed to successfully
implement the GSAC program. We have secured and are in the process of agreeing with other important
donors, including the IMF, DFID, USAID, UNDP and EU-TACIS, that will provide grant-based TA for
several activities that support the GSAC reform agenda. We are also seeking TA from the World Bank
for the remaining activities, through the Governance Technical Assistance Credit (GTAC), which
accompanies the GSAC.
L      Macroeconomic Policy Framework
5.     We realize that maintaining macroeconomic stability and growth, reducing external debt and
achieving further gains in poverty reduction requires continuation of fiscal and external adjustment;
further progress in structural reforms; and continued support from external creditors, both in the form of
further debt rescheduling and increased grant and concessional financing. An IMF Poverty Reduction
and Growth Facility Arrangement (PRGF), approved in December 2001, provides the basic



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  47
underpinnings for this policy framework. The implementation of this program has so far remained on
track. We have met and in specific cases even exceeded performance benchmarks.
6.     Our reform efforts have also been supported through adjustment lending by the World Bank.
Seven Bank adjustment operations supported privatization and enterprise reform, agricultural
privatization, financial sector reform, public sector resource management, pension reform and power and
gas sector reform along with improvements in the business environment. These reforms helped our
country to undertake reforms to orient its policy and institutional framework to market economy
principles, although reforms are far from complete. The GSAC program will help us take this reforms
further and consolidate our prospects for growth and poverty reduction.
11.    The Government's Program to Increase the Effectiveness of the State
7.     We believe that the quality of governance will be a key determinant of the success of our country
in achieving sustainable growth and poverty reduction. The State would need to play a major role in the
implementation of the National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NPRS). It must formulate pro-poor policies
and maintain macro-economic stability; spur sustainable growth; protect the vulnerable; encourage the
emergence of a vibrant private sector; and promote a free, open and just society, where citizens have the
opportunity to achieve their potential. The State must also muster the requisite financial, human and
physical resources needed to put the policies into effect. Finally, it must ensure that the policies are
implemented effectively and their benefits actually reach the poor. To discharge these responsibilities,
the State would need to have the requisite technical and administrative capacity. But even more
important, it would need to operate in a manner that inspires trust and galvanizes the people around the
goal of poverty reduction. This would be possible only if the State is sharply focused on promoting the
interests of the poor, its policies are formulated and executed in a transparent manner, the voice of the
people is heard and the agents of the State are held accountable for their actions.
8.     We have highlighted some of the problems for the effective functioning of the state in the NSPR.
At the political level, the electoral system needs to be improved, political parties are weak and the
communication between the State and the people is ineffective. The legal framework is still in the
process of development and suffers from outdated norms, high frequency of change, lack of public
discussion of proposed laws and regulations and inadequate public access to normative acts. The
judiciary and law enforcement agencies face serious capacity and integrity challenges, that reduce their
ability to uphold the law. At the level of central and local administration, policy making capacity is
extremely weak, implementation of policies is uneven, transparency and accountability are inadequate
and Government interference in business activities is excessive. Local self Governments lack adequate
functional and fiscal authority, management capacity and community participation. Public expenditure
management reforms need to be deepened to st-engthen budget formulation, budget execution and
financial accountability. Substantial improvements are needed in the system of inter-Governmental
finance. The level of political interference and instability in the civil service remains high, while its
professional capacity and accountability for performance remain low. The structure of Government needs
further rationalization. Corruption is a major systemic problem that plagues many public agencies, as
confirmed by surveys of households and firms.
9.     We recognize that weaknesses of the Slate will pose a serious hurdle in the country's
development path and in its effort to alleviate poverty. For this reason, we give a very high priority to the
formation of an effective state through a multi-faceted strategy laid out in the NSPR.
10.    For the purpose of the GSAC, we have selected a subset of high priority reforms, which fall under
two mutually reinforcing categories. First, reforms designed to enhance the transparency, openness and
accountability of the public sector to external stakeholders. Second, reforms aimed at improving



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural A djushrnent Credit- Pngrwn Docum E n t,, April 21, 2003  48
e Ffectiveness and efficiency within the public sector. We realize that strengthening the effectiveness of
the State will be a long process, requiring sustairind efforts spread of over several years. This is due to the
inherent complexity of institutional reforms that seek to bring about behavioral changes, the large number
of exogenous variables that could affect progress and the evidently weak institutional capacity of our
State at present. Therefore, improvements in governance vill be realized only slowly and incremental[y.
Yet, given the centrality of good governance to cLevelopment and poverty reduction, it is imperative for us
to undertake the next steps in the reform process. Together vith the World Bank team we are making
efforts to keep the level of sophistication of the rr;forms withini achievable limits. As such, the
Government is reasonably confident that, given adiequate technical assistance, it would be able to
implement the second generation reforms.
A.     IMPROVE THE TRANSPARENCY AND RESPONSIVENESS OF THE PUBLIC
SECTOR AND ENHIANCE THE ABILITY OF EXTERNAL STAKEHOLDERS
TO HOLD IT ACCOUNTABLE
(i)    Improving Personal Accountability of Political Officials and Civil Servants
11.    We are committed to increase the accourrability of persons in authority, in order to combat
corruption. For this purpose we will improve the effectiveness of the system of declaration of income and
assets as one of the instruments used to promote integrity amnongst political leaders and civil servants who
exercise state authority. Currently, the Election Code, the Law on KR Jogorku Kenesh Deputies' Status
and Civil Service Law22 have some provisions for declaration of income and assets by candidates,
deputies to Parliament and civil servants, respectively, but these need to be improved and expanded.
None of the laws requires publication of the declarations. The declarations filed by Deputies and civil
servants are explicitly treated as secret. Also, these officials are not required to file any declarations
regarding the income and assets of their close farrily members. The S1Fate Revenue Committee, which
receives the declarations of deputies and civil servants does not have the capacity to systematically
scrutinize these to identify cases of illicit enrichment.
12.     For this purpose, we propose to take the following measures. The Government has issued
decrees indicating its intention to submit to Parliament (a) a draft Law on Declaration and Publication of
Income and Assets of High State Officials in the E,xecutive, .Judicial arid Legislative Branches of the
State, including the President, Prime Minister, Vice Prime Ministers, Mlinisters, Deputies Ministers,
Judges and members of the Parliament and their close familv members; and (b) amendments to the Civil
Service Law, satisfactory to IDA, to (i) require declaration of iincome and assets of civil servants and their
close family members and (ii) make the declaraticns of incornes and assets of civil servants available to
the public, and declarations of incomes and assets of their close family members available to specifiecl
state bodies23. Before second tranche release, the Law on Declaration and Publication of Income and
Assets of High State Officials and the aforesaid amnendments to the Civil Service Law will be enacted.
Before third tranche, we will ensure publication of declarations of income and assets of High State
Officials and their close family members, in accordance with the aforesaid Law. We will also make the
declarations of income and assets of civil servants available, on reque.,t, to the public. Appropriate
administrative arrangements, to ensure regular, efiective examination of the declarations of civil servants
and their close family members will be implemented to detect conflict of interest and illicit enrichment.

Article 13, the Law on Civil Service, 1999.

tu)    Strengthening Voice and Participation by Improving Access to Official Infornation.
13.    We realize that the availability of relevant and timely information is an important pre-requisite for
enabling civil society, the media and the public to contribute to policy dialogue, participate in consultative
processes and oversee the performance of public sector entities. We have made a lot of efforts to create
an open Government, increase the transparency of the public sector and increase public engagement in
Govemmental activities. For example, public budget hearings were held to receive feedback on annual
budget for 2002. The budget and the Fiscal Bulletin are published. Web sites of several Govemrnment
entities have been created to disseminate information on the intemet.
14.   In the NPRS we place a high priority on strengthening civic participation in budget formulation
and execution at all levels of Govemment and in other decisions involving general community interests.
We also aim to increase the ability of civil society to monitor activities of state agencies. For this
purpose, we will improve public access to information on activities of state agencies and local authorities.
In accordance with agreements with IDA, we have already published annual audited financial Statements
and audit reports of 10 largest joint stock companies with State share of 51 percent or more, for FY 2001;
information on public procurement contract awards exceeding $20,000 made in 2002, the complete
annual audit report of the Chamber of Accounts on the execution of the republican budget for FY 2001, as
adopted by the Parliament; the list of firms receiving tax concessions and exemptions, as an investment
incentive; and the list of 50 largest delinquent taxpayers and energy users. We have also organized a
workshop to disseminate the "Governance and Service Delivery" report prepared by the World Bank,
based on service delivery survey conducted in 2001. By second tranche, we will institute mechanisms,
satisfactory to IDA, for the regular publication of the documents mentioned above, including information
about the public sector reform program implementation and results of ongoing monitoring and evaluation
of the health sector reforms.
15.    Before third tranche release we will: (i) enact amendments to the Law on Guarantees and
Freedom of Access to Information, satisfactory to IDA, to enhance access of the public to official
information, strengthen the mechanisms of individuals to appeal against denials of requests for
information, and provide a system for monitoring of compliance of state bodies with the Law. We will
also establish a Government Public Information Center to provide free access to official information of
public interest and to the portal and websites of the Government.
(iii)  Improving the Interaction between Regulatory Agencies and Business Enterprises
16.    Our Government is placing particular importance to the development of a competitive business
environment as one of the cornerstones to achieve growth and poverty reduction in the Kyrgyz Republic.
However, the interaction between the state and enterprises suffers from various problems that dampen the
development of the private sector. Many regulatory agencies are partially financed by 'special means
funding', which includes fees from licenses, permits, certificates and fines levied for infraction of
regulations. This creates incentives for regulatory excesses. To prevent misuse of regulatory and
enforcement powers, through a Presidential Decree, the Govemment has taken a decision to prohibit
formation and use of special means funding of Govemmental institutions through receipts from issue of
permits, licenses and certificates and fines imposed through inspections, except as specifically allowed by
law. License fees were eliminated from special means funding from the fiscal year 2002. We have
issued a decree to prohibit formation and use of special means funding of Governmental institutions
through fees from issue of permits. A similar action will be taken, before second tranche, with regard to
fees from issue of certificates and receipts from imposition of fines resulting from inspections. Before
second tranche, we will execute the Budget on the basis that fees from issue of licenses, permits and other
similar requirements placed on economic activities, are directed only to the State budget and not credited
to the Special Means Accounts of regulatory agencies, except for the State Energy Agency (SEA) and the

National Communications Agency (NCA). By third tranche, fees froJn certificates and receipts from
irnposition of fines resulting from inspections would also he directed to the State budget. T'7us, by third
tranche we would have succesoslly implementfd a aset f vJle,gislative rind other measures, satisfactory to
IDA, to ensure thatfees, firom Issue of licenses, permits, ec efifates and other similar requirem7ents
