and not credited to the Special Means Accounts cf regularaty ogencies, with the exception of SEA and
NCA, and that regulatory agencies are providexl c lonipensijtor) fulncing through the State budget.
17.    On the normative side, there is multiplscity of laws and reguiations affecting businesses, many of
which are outdated. There is inadequate partic i pation of the private sector in development of new laws
and regulations. Little assessrrient of the impac: of new regulatory instruments on the business
environment is carried out before they are put into effect. 'rhis also encourages proliferation of
regulations. To rectify these deficiencies, we have formed an inter-departmental comnission in the
Ministry of Justice to review proposals for improvement of legislation. We have also reviewed more than
150 laws and 30 Presidential decrees. Amendments to the Law on Nornative Legal Acts that require that
draft normative legal acts regulating or otherwise restricting entrepreneurial entities should be made
public, publicly discussed, harmonized and studied for their economic, social and budgetary impact
before being enacted, have been submitted to th- Parliament. Before second tranche release, we will
enact these amendments to the Law otn Nonmativ e LUgal Acts and, by the third tranche, its provisions will
be fully implemnented for all Acts, issued after the passage of the Lavw There will also be a requirement
for publishing brief information about draft Act:s proposed to be enacted, in the state gazette.
Furthermore, we will conduct Regulatory Impact Assessments of three new Laws and three new
regulations, to be agreed with IDA, in accordance with the new Law.
18.    Regulatory agencies continue to have overlapping authority, as a result of which businesses face
multiple inspections by different agencies for si rnilar purposes. Private firms are often unaware of the
regulatory norms applicable to them, as well as their rights and obligations when they are subjected to
inspections by state agencies. The mechanisms for addressing grievances of enterprises against abuse of
authority by regulatory agencies are weak. To address th.ese problenms, before the second tranche, we will
connect the database of the Ministry of Justice, which contains draft laws, enacted laws, regulations and
instructions, to the internet to make it publicly available. In addition, by third tranche, we will enact a
Law on Administrative Procedures to, inter alia, institute specific procedures for internal administrative
review of all actions which impose a penalty or burden upon, or deny a request sought by a citizen or
legal entity, and provide the right of appeal to a court, after exhaustion of administrative appeals. Before
second tranche release, we will also develop and implement a plan for rationalizing public health related
inspections carried out by the Sanitary and Epidemiology Services (SES) and for rationalizing the
pharmaceutical inspections canied out by the Pharmaceuticxl C'ontrol Laboratory, publicizing their rules,
regulations and results, improving oversight by .he Ministry of Health and creating effective grievance
mechanisms against regulatory abuse. Further, iefore second tranche release, we will develop a plan for
rationalization of the functions and reorganization of Kyrgyzstandarct, satisfactory to IDA. The
implementation of the plan for rationalizing of t`ie functiorns and reotganization of Kyrgyzstandard will be
implemented prior to third tranche.
(iv)   Strengthening External Audit
19.    An effective, independent external audit is esseniial for enforcing accountability in the public
sector and enabling the Parliament to oveisee the working of the executive. Our Government has made
significant efforts to improve external audit, wit i previous support from the World Bank, including
clarification of responsibilities among the varioLcs audit institutions sujch as the Chamber of Accounts
(CA) - the equivalent of a Supreme Audit Instit, tion, the State Financial Inspectorate (SFI) under the
Prime Minister's office and Internal Audit Department in the Ministry of Finance. Though the Law on



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  51
the Chamber of Accounts24, we have established a 13-member CA, reporting both to the President and the
Parliament. In 2000, we merged the SFI with the Chamber of Accounts to avoid duplication of
functions. We are aware, however, that the external audit function continues to have deficiencies, as
detailed in a recent Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CFAA) conducted by the World Bank.
20.    The independence of the CA needs to be strengthened. In addition to the funds received by the
republican budget, the CA is also entitled to receive 10% of the amount recovered to the budget as a result
of irregularities detected by it. This financing arrangement leads to perverse incentives. While the Law
requires the CA to 'control' a large number of financial transactions, it does not require it to prepare a
comprehensive report on the audit of the annual financial statements of the Government. There are also
no requirements regarding the adoption of audit standards. The majority of the staff in the Chamber of
Accounts was transferred from the SFI. The staff, though proficient in conducting compliance audits,
lacks skills in and exposure to financial audits.
21.    As we have emphasized in our NPRS we are commnitted to improving external audit to strengthen
accountability and transparency in Government spending. With this end in view, before release of the
second tranche, we will enact amendments to the Law on the Chamber of Accounts, satisfactory to
IDA,would be enacted to inter alia, (i) ensure that the Budget Committee of the Parliament has an
opportunity to review, jointly with the Ministry of Finance, the draft budget of the Chamber of Accounts
prior to its submission to the Government; (ii) eliminate the provision entitling the Chamber of Accounts
to retain 10 percent of the amounts recovered to the budget as a result of its activity, and provide
compensatory funding through the State budget; and (iii) adopt international audit standards acceptable
to the Association. From the Fiscal year 2005 onwards, the Budget Committee of the Parliament will
review, jointly with the Ministry of Finance, the draft budget of the Chamber of Accounts prior to its
submission to the Government.
B. INCREASE EFFICIENCY, EFFECTIVENESS AND ACCOUNTABILITY WITHIN THE
PUBLIC SECTOR
(i)    Strengthening Public Expenditure Management
22.    We believe that a transparent, well-performing public finance system is essential for good
governance and effective economic management. Since independence, we have made important progress
in establishing the basic elements of public expenditure management (PEM) institutions, including a legal
framework and procedures for budget management, a basic framework for inter governmental finance
relation, a Treasury system -the first among CIS, and an external audit function. These reforms,
however, are only a first step in a long and continuous process. Several issues remain to be addressed to
ensure the sustainability and effective functioning of the PEM system. We continue to have ineffective
and unpredictable budgets as a result of a weak budget policy formulation process. The implementation
of the inter governmental finance system established in 1997 has been inadequate. There are also
weaknesses in budget execution given the limitations associated with a manual Treasury system and
underdeveloped Internal Control and External Audit systems. We are committed, however, to address all
these fronts and have given high priority to this agenda in our NPRS.
24 Law on the Chamber of Accounts of the Kyrgyz Republic. 1998.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structura I Adjustnent Credit - Program Docum ent,, April.'1, 2003  52
(a)    Establishing a more Strategic and Trmnsparent Budget Formulation Process
23.    Our main objective of the reformLs in t:tis area will be to establish a more strategic and open
budget policy formulation. We will aim to improve the linkrages between the policy planning and
allocation of resources and establish the budgel as the main strategic policy instrument for formulating,
irnplementing and monitoring public programs. By strengthening the policy focus of the budget, we will
aim to establish a framework for prioritization of public expenditure programs, and open the way for
integrating the objectives of the CDF and NSPRZ within the budget. A more transparent and strategic
policy formulation process is also likely to create a buffer against pressures from vested interests, which
become difficult to resist in a policy vacuum.
24.    We have intensified eiforts towards more strategic budgeting in the last three years. We are
making good progress with the preparation of a simplified version of a Medium Term Budget Framework
(MTBF) for 2004-2006. However, a substantial agenda remains to expand and fully institutionalize the
MTBF process as the main instrument for Government's budget pol Icy formulation. Initial actions that
we will undertake include (i) consolidation of thie economic policy decision making process; (ii)
improvements in the format for presenting the budget to the Parliament; and (iii) better integration of the
recurrent and investment planning processes.
25.    Integrating the decision making process: Eco:nomic policy and budget decision making is
fragmented between the Budget Commnission w hich reviews and approves the recurrent budget and the
GOSKOMINVEST Committee which reviews and approves the Public Investment Program (PIP) and the
Development Budget (annual investment plan),. Subsequently. both budgets are consolidated (as
aggregates) and sent to the entire cabinet for approval. This practice can lead to incoherent decisions with
regard to macro and fiscal discipline, sector policies and resource allocation, which in tum can undermnine
the effectiveness of the MTBF.
26.    We have recently established an apex I vel Econocrruc Policy Council (EPC) for strengthening
economic policy management in the country and are now considering merging the budget related
functions of the Goskominvesi and the Budget Commission into the EPC. This will allow our
Government to ensure consistency in economic policy. It will also ensure that overall macroeconomic
and fiscal policy decisions are taken at a point in the policy-.-making process that allows the Governmaent
to impose fiscally sound, realistic and binding budget const-aints and make policy trade-offs. By second
tranche, we will implement appropriate actions, satisfactorv to IDA, for integrating the functions of the
existing Budget Commission and the budget relatedfunctions of the Goskominvest in the Economic Policy
Council.
27.    Integrating recurrent and investment budget pl[atnning processes: At present investment and
recurrent budgets are planned separately to the extent that they are often driven by different objectives
and little consideration of recurrent cost implications of in vestment decisions. Moreover, there is very
little integration between domestic investment planning and foreign investment planning, as the majority
of the investment budget is financed by donors. Budget c lassificaticin structures are not consistent
between recurrent and investment budgets. We are committed to undertake several measures to better
integrate the budget planning process. By third tranche, estimates of recurrent cost implications of all
investment projects will be included in the Medium Term 'Budget Framework (MBTF), beginning with
MTBF for 2005-2007.
28.    Enabling the Parliament to review budget policy priorities: The format in which we present
the annual budget to Parliament is geared towards the economic expenditure items and agency
expenditure levels, rather than sectoral and inter-sectoral policies and priorities. Such format not only



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  53
makes it difficult for the Parliament to see what policies we are proposing to finance, but also creates
confusion about the authority that can authorize changes in the budget. It requires Parliament to approve
even unimportant changes in the course of budget implementation, distracting its attention from major
policy issues. Before second tranche, we will revise the format for presentation to the Parliament of the
annual budget law, with the view to (i) make more transparent the policy priorities financed by the
budget, and; (ii) present sectoral ceilings for two consecutive years, based on the MTBF; and (iii)
integrate recurrent and investment expenditures. By third tranche, the Government will present to
Parliament its annual budget law for 2005, in the revisedformat.
(b)    Improving Effectiveness of Inter-Governmental Finances
29.    We have made some progress in establishing an inter governmental finance system since 1997.
More recently progress has been made also in the area of administrative decentralization with elections of
local self Govemments (lowest tier of Government) in December 2001. Implementation of the reforms,
however, has been erratic, especially with regard to the ad hoc shifting of expenditure responsibilities to
sub-regional Governments, calculation and allocation of categorical and equalization grants and the
extension of the rules for revenue sharing at the lower levels of Government. Back sliding in several
fronts has produced a seriously distorted structure that fails to produce desired outcomes.
30.    We fully recognize the importance of addressing weaknesses in the implementation of inter
governmental finances and are committed to embark on a program for improving various elements of the
system.
31.    Making the existing rules transparent: Non-transparent application of the existing rules and
procedures for allocating resources to the regional and especially sub-regional Governments has created
room for negotiated, unstable, discretionary and unpredictable budgets at the local level. Local
Governments, especially below the Oblast level, are not aware of how categorical and equalization grants
are allocated to them from the higher levels. In addition, various expenditure responsibilities are shifted
to them during budget implementation, especially in cases where they are able to collect more revenues
than planned. Such practices allow room for inefficiencies, discretion and rent seeking in the course of
allocating and executing the budget. To make transparent all the existing rules and procedures, as well as
the methodology used by the Ministry of Finance to allocate resources to the local Governments, before
third tranche, the new rules and procedures for the calculation of all transfers between different levels of
government will be developed, published, and made available to all interested parties, especially local
Govemments at all levels.
32.    Establishing stable tax sharing at the sub-regional level: The Law on the Basic Principles of
the Budget (1997) defines a tax sharing system between the Central Government and the Oblast
Govemments. However, below the Oblast level tax sharing is very unsystematic and usually subject to
negotiations of rayons and cities with the Oblast level, with perhaps some small improvements in
Bishkek City and Jalal-Abad oblast. Such a system does not assure any stability or predictability for the
sub-regional Governments. In addition, these negotiations and the level of discretion that the Oblast has
to allocate resources to the sub-regional levels, totally undermine the role of elected local self
Govemments. Recently, the government has extended the tax sharing system to the rayon level in
Bishkek City and Jalal-Abad. We have also extended the tax sharing system to the rayon level in three
additional oblasts.
(c)    Strengthening Accountability and Transparency in Budget Execution
33.    Our main objective of the reforms on budget execution will be to reduce discretion and non-
transparent practices in the allocation and disbursement of the budget funds, by improving financial



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structar7l Adjustnent Credit -- Program Docament,, April 21, 2003  54
planning and cash managemeat, bringing all tire accounts of the bucdget institutions under the Treasury
control and phasing out non-cash settlements. We believe that this will not only make badget execution
more transparent, but it will also improve the predictability of funding policy programs and activities.
34.    Improving financial planning and cash management: The introduction of a manual Treasury
system in 1996 substantially improved budget execution. lHowever, several weaknesses have emerged,
which undermine our Government's capacities to implemeiit policies and programs and create
possibilities for extracting rents and favors during budget execution. The disbursement of budget ftnds
(spending authorization process) has resulted in an ad hoc system of daily negotiations between the
budget department, Treasury ,and spending agencies to finance mainly protected expenditure items25. This
comes from the lack of a proper system for financial planning and cash management, which is essential in
a country with very limited financial resources and an uneven flow of liquidity in the Government
accounts during the year. The existence of special means accounts and other accounts outside the
Treasury General Ledger (TGL) also inhibits eood financial planning and cash management during
budget implementation.
35.    We recognize these issues and are taking measurcs to addre ss them. We have already createcl a
new Financial Planning department within the Treasury andi are working on streamrlining the MOF
procedures for authorizing spending. We have also adepted a regulationthat establishes a new procedlure
for the preparation of the monthly financial plan. The new procedures for financial planning will be
implemented before release of the second tranche.
36.    Phasing out extra budgetary operations of Budget Institutions: Lack of appropriate cash
management systems in the MOF and the tight controls imlposed on the budget institutions in the use of
cash resources have encouraged the emergence of fiscal operations outside the Governrment budget. in
violation of the Law on the KR Treasury. Over 40 percen-t of the aggregate extra budgetary resources at
the end of 200026 were held outside the Treasury Single Account (rSA), probably in cash or other bank
accounts. Almost every central Budget Institution maintains a so called "Special Means Accounts",
which, until recently, was operating outside thie TSA. The amount of money deposited in these accounts
in some cases is equal to 100 percent of a budget institution's budget allocation.
37.    We have initiated various actions to control the opening of such accounts, including the
reiteration of the basic provisions in the Annual Budget Law for 2002. However, we realize that a more
systematic approach needs to be adopted with regard to conitrolling extra budgetary fiscal operations of
any form. We will adopt an Action Plan, sati..factory to iDA, for transferring all Extra-budgetary
Accounts, with the exception of the Social Funtd, into the 7reasury General Ledger and make satisfactory
progress in its implementation. By third tranchie, the transfer of all Evxtra-budgetary Accounts, with the
exception of the Social Fund, into the Treasury General Ledger will be completed..
38.    Phasing out off-set transactions in the Treasury: There is a widespread use of off-set
transactions within the Treasury for paying utilities and some contractors, which has reached the
magnitude of around 25-30 percent of the annual budget (pFactically all the utility payments). These off-
sets are legitimized in the Treasury Manual, which permnits the settlement of dues between the
Government and the suppliers of goods and seurvices on a non-cash barter basis. While we have designed
special accounting rules to record these transactions, they present inappropriate budget managemenit
practices and might conceal liquidity problems in the real sectors, such as energy, for paying tax liabilities
to the Government's budget. Moreover they undermnine firnancial planning since the amounts dedicated to
off-sets become by default non-discretionary spending and can not be reallocated. We will issue a



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - P'rogram Document,, April 21, 2003  55
resolution, adopting an Action Plan for phasing out off-sets and non-cash transactions in the budget and
the Social Fund, satisfactory to IDA, by second tranchie. By third tranche, we will make satisfactory
progress in the implementation of the Action Plan.
39.    Establishing systematic reconciliation between the Treasury records on payments made and
reimbursements received from the agent banks. At present there is no reconciliation between the
Treasury records on payments made during the day on behalf of the Govemment and the amount of
reimbursement to the agent banks for these transactions. This process creates possibilities for the agent
banks to over-report on payments, be over-reimbursed from the National Bank of KR and utilize the float
during the month for their own purposes. Apart from the ineffective management of the Government's
idle funds, such practice may create possibilities for non-transparent use of the funds on the bank's side as
well as conceal difficulties they might have in meeting reserve requirements. To address these problems,
the MOF has recently implemented a systematic rmechanism for daily reconciliation of Treasury
transactions and reimbursements from the NBKR.
(iv)   Improving the Internal Conttol Environment
40.    We realize that the Internal Audit function in the Kyrgyz Republic is underdeveloped. The
Ministry of Finance continues to maintain a small internal audit unit to carry out compliance tests for
treasury transactions. However, currently, there are no internal audit units within the line ministries,
except in the Ministry of Labor and Social Protection. There is a lack of clear legal obligations requiring
line ministries to maintain an adequate internal control framework. Ad hoc steps taken to ensure financial
probity have proved to be ineffective in preventing abuse and waste of resources, as is indicated by
several instances of non-compliance with laws and fraud and corruption reported by the CA in its annual
reports.
41.    We recognize the need to strengthen the internal control environment within the public sector.
Therefore, by second tranche, we will develop a Strategy for the creation of an effective intemal audit
function in public entities. By third tranche release, we will issue an appropriate legal instrument, to
implement the Strategy. We will also implement the Strategy in at least three Ministries, by the third
tranche.
(v)    Imnproving Transparency, Value for Money and Accountability in Public Procurement
42.    We are committed to improve substantially the procurement of goods, services and works which
constitute about 35% of budgetary expenditures. We realize that an open, competitive procurement
process, has the potential of increasing the efficiency of this substantial amount of public spending. At
the same time, improvements are important to increase the transparency and accountability in
procurement, as this is an area where the risk of corruption is very high.
43.    In 1997, we enacted a Public Procurement Law (PPL) conform international standards. This law
established a decentralized procurement system, under which procurement is carried out by individual
public entities; created an independent oversight body (the State Procurement Agency), with authority to
issue regulations; and established modern procurement processes. However, as indicated by the Country
Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR), conducted in 2002, the procurement system continues to suffer
from various organizational, normative, procedural, financial and capacity deficiencies.
44.    The State Procurement Agency was mer-ged with the State Material Reserves Fund in 2001, to
form the State Commission on Public Procurement and Material Reserves (SCPPMR). This has created a
potential conflict of interest, as the agency is now responsible for both procuring for the Reserves and for



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Docuiment,, April-'1,2003  56
supervising itself in connection with those procurements. The organizational structure of the SCPPMR
needs to be improved to enhance its capacity in legal, monitoring and evaluation, and procurement
capacity building functions. The funding mechanisms of the SCPPMR also need to be changed. While
part of the costs of the agency are met by budgetary allocations, it is also entitled to receive 1% of
notional savings made on contracts concluded lhrough unlimited tenders, creating conflict of interest. By
second tranche, the Government will prepare anid implernent a restructuring plan, satisfactory to IDA, for
the State Commission on Public Procurement and Material Reserves (SCPPMR), to separate the
procurement function from the functions of the State Material Reserves, within the SCPPMR; strengthen
the legal, monitoring and evaluation and procurement capacity building functions of the SPA; and
strengthen the capacity of procuring entities.
45.    There has been a proliferation of procurement rules issued by different authorities. Since 1998,
26 decrees, instructions and orders, dealing with different aspects of procurement, have been issued.
Many of these rules have led to confusion in th. division of responsibilities and distortions in
procurement practices. There appear to be frequent deviations from procurement procedures established
by the PPL, for instance, procuring entities frecluently disclose estirriated contract values to bidders,
extend bid submission deadlines informally and sometimes negotiate contact prices with selected bidders.
Although the PPL does not stipulate it, the SCPIPMR has assumed a direct role in procurement through
review of bidding documents, evaluation repor.s, contracts, etc. This makes the procuring entities
excessively dependent on the SCPPMR and seriously compromises the latter's oversight and supervision
responsibilities.
46.    The efficiency and cost effectiveness of public procurement is also seriously affected by
deficiencies in budget execution. Public agencies enter into contractual commitments based on their
approved budget allocations. But due to cash flow constraints of the Treasury, they do not receive the
required funds in a timely manner. Therefore, payments to contractors are frequently delayed. This
discourages enterprises from bidding for Government contracts, thus, limiting competition. It also raises
the cost of goods and services procured, as contractors factor the cost of potential payment delays in their
bid prices.
47.    There is no training strategy or prograrn for providing procurement training to public servants on
a regular basis. As a result, procurement is being conducted largely by untrained and unqualified public
officials.
48.    In order to deal with the aforesaid deficiencies, the Government has already confrmned its
agreement with the CPAR Action Plan. By second trancht, we wili implement the activities pertaining to
2003 included in the CPAR Action Plan, dated December 24, 2002. By third tranche, the implementation
of the remaining activities of the CPAR Action Plan will be completed. In addition, we will prepare and
implement a Procurement Training Strategy and introduce an attestation program for Procurement
Officers, to raise the qualification level of persons engaged in procurement.
49.    In order to enhance accountability and transparency in the public procurement, before second
and third tranche, we will arrange to carry out procurement audits of selected, high value items, to be
agreed with IDA, that affect service quality in the Health arnd Education sectors (e.g. selected
pharmaceuticals on the List of Essential Drugs, text books etc.). The audits will verify compliance with
the procurement rules and regulations, assess value for mcnrey with reference to international and national
market prices and recommend consequential remedial actions. The procurement audit reports will be
made public.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003          57
(iv)    Improving Service Delivery in the Health, Education and Social Protection Sectors
50.     The health sector of the Kyrgyz Republic, similar to those of other transition economies, is facing
the challenge of providing quality health services to the population in the context of shrinking public
expenditures on health care, an excessive number of health facilities and personnel and a weak emphasis
on cost-effective preventive and primary health care approaches. To address these problems, since 1996,
the Kyrgyz Republic has been implementing a comprehensive health sector reform program, in a phased
manner27. Key elements of the reform program include: (i) rationalizing health facilities and personnel;
(ii) improving equity in the geographical allocation of resources, (iii) making the health system more cost-
effective by strengthening preventive and primary health care services, (iv) making providers accountable
for efficiency, quality and access at the health facility level, and (iv) reducing corruption in the health
sector. New health care financing (the single payer reform28), was introduced in two regions in 2001 and
in two more regions in 2002. It extends the output-based purchasing methods of the Mandatory Health
Insurance Fund (MIEIF) to budget funds and also includes the formalization of hospital co-payments.
Implementation progress on health reforms is satisfactory, but critical challenges remain29. In particular,
the organizational changes in primary care have not been supported by a corresponding shift in resource
allocation from the tertiary to the primary level.
51.     During 2002, implementation of health reforms slowed down as there was little progress in the
expansion of the single-payer system to other oblasts, and the flow of funds into the MHIF and the single-
payer system was seriously jeopardized. These problems faced by the health reforms have been discussed
at the highest levels of the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic and in February, 2003, at a Roundtable
organized by the MOH with the help of the World bank, WHO and other donors, the President of the
Kyrgyz Republic expressed his strong support for the refonms. The resolution based on this Roundtable
has been adopted by the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic. A time-bound action plan based on this
Resolution has been developed. The action plan highlights the importance of timely and adequate flow of
funds in the health sector and the further expansion and strengthening of the single-payer reformns in the
Kyrgyz Republic. We will implement the Action Plan and closely monitor and publish the results. In the
context of the GSAC the Government will pursue specific reforms in the health, education and social
protection sectors as discussed below.
52.     Single Payer Law: Implementation of recent reforms in the health sector and especially the
introduction of the Single Payer System requires changes in institutional arrangements for budget
management. As noted above, these changes relate to the output-based purchasing and pooling of health
resources. This new practice is quite different from the current process of input based budgeting, where
health providers (e.g. hospitals) are financed on the basis of physical norms (number of beds, staff).
Although some changes in budget management practices for the health sector have taken place in selected
Oblasts, these are based on temporary regulations rather than legislation, and this puts the health sector
reform process in a rather tenuous position. Another problem, especially in those oblasts that are
implementing the formal co-payment policy for hospitalizations, is unplanned and non-transparent re-
allocations from health to other sectors. Therefore, suitable changes need to be made in the legislative
basis for budget management and other reforms in the health sector, while ensuring that these are
consistent with overall budget management and financial accountability systems. By second tranche, we
will enact a Single-payer Law, satisfactory to IDA.
n The Manas Health Reform Program.
7 The single payer system refers to an arrangement where all funds at the oblast level (from oblast, rayon and city) are collected within a single
organization (TDMHIF). The purpose of the single payer reform is to reduce the fragmentation and duplication among the various levels (oblast,
rayon and city) and streamline the financing and delivery of health services. Since one payer is responsible for contracting of health services, this
reduces administrative costs and also strengthens the ability of the MHIF to offer a coherent set of incentives to providers.
29 The recently completed Kyrgyz Republic Public Expenditure Review discusses these challenges is greater details



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit- "rogre avL' cDz.mlent, April 21, 2003    58
53.     Establishing the Bishkek Territorial Health Insurance Fund: Despite the progress in the
health sector, there have been few changes in the allocation of public funds towards primary care and
regions with the greatest healtht needs. For example, hospitals consurne 70 percent of public expenditures,
and the geographical allocatiorn of resources is s kewed w~ ith Bishkek being favored over the other regions.
Improvements in allocative efficiency and geographical ecluity of pu5lic funds are needed to improve the
poverty impact of public expenditures on healtir care. Rea location is contingent on the implementation
of the single-payer system in atl oblasts and, especially, in Bishkek .:' Before second tranche, we will
issue a decree, satisfactory to IDA, establishing the l3ishikek Territorial Health Insurance Fund and the
rulds and regulations guiding the operation of the Fund.
54.     Financing of Healthcare Insurance ftur Pensioners and the Unemployed: Since its
establishment in 1997, the MIHF has faced the lchallenge of timely and adequate transfer of funds from
the Social Fund (SF) for employed persons and pensioners and from the Republican budget for children
and persons with social benefits3t. Amnong all categories, transfers for pensioners is the most problematic.
For example, between 1997-2002, only 18% of planned revenues for pensioners has been transferredl to
the MHIF. Pensioners are generally heavy users of health services, and the fact that the MHIF does not
receive adequate funding for tlhs is one of contiibuting factors for MHIF financial stress. By second
tranche, we will amend current legislation to esi-ablish responsibility of the Republican budget for the
financing of allocations for healthcare insurance of pensioners and the unemployed.. The 2005 budget
will include funds for this expenditure item.
55.     Rationalizing Functional and Expenditure Responsibilities in Health and Education
Sectors: In order to advance reforms in health and educa,ion, there is a need to have a fresh look at how
expenditure responsibilities can be streamlined and made stable among various Govemment levels to
ensure better service delivery. The Government will, by second tranche, prepare draft amendments ito the
relevant legislation, satisfactory to IDA, to streamline and clearly define functional and expenditure
responsibilities in the health sector. By the third tranche, we will arrend legislation, satisfactory to IDA,
to streamline and clearly define functional and expenditure responsibilities in the health sector. We will
also amend legislation, satisfactory to IDA, to streamdine and clearly define functional and expenditure
responsibilities in the education sector, by third tranche.
56.     Establishing a system for defining minimun serice standards in Health and Education
Sectors: In order to assure local fulfillment of regional and national priorities, minimum service
requirements must be defined for priority servic es. Withouit this, there is no basis for determining
appropriate financing, or holding sub-national Governments accountable for the proper execution of
expenditure responsibilities. To the degree that standards currently exist in the Kyrgyz Republic, they are
based on physical and facility norms and are not conducive to assuring any meaningful level of
accomplishment. Especially ir the health sector, where a different f.nancing and service provision system
is being implemented, the use of inputs and ph sical norms as a means for allocating expenditures has
proven outdated. It is important therefore, to start the development of service standards. Before second
tranche, we will issue decrees defining the methodology for developing minimum service standards in
the education sector and refining those in the health sector.
57.     Categorical Grants for Health and Education: Ann .mportant problem affecting basic service
delivery is local Government contributions to the health sector, espe:ially from categorical grants (CG).
The resource allocation formula under the CG is linked to inputs (mainly salaries) and therefore
30 Implementation of the single payer systemin Bish-kek is needed to create incentives for supouly-side rationalization whichis needed to reduce
the overall envelope of pubbc spending on Bishkek health faci1iii,s Rationalization mi Bishkek is supposed to free-up resources for improving
the geographical equity and increase spenc'ing ODn public health, preventlve and priray healt), care services.
31 The recently completed Kyrgyz Republbc Public Expcnditure Review disctusses the.e challenges is greater details.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit- Program Document,, April 21, 2003  59
negatively affects health sector allocations which are increasingly moving towards output-based
allocations. The design of CGs needs to be synchronized with the current developments in the health
system. In the education sector, the practice of financing teacher salary costs under the Republican
budget treats existing teacher costs as non-discretionary and hence tends to legitimate what may be an
inefficient delivery of education. A preferred basis for central financing of education costs is through a
differentiated capitation system of financing, which pays for education on the basis of the number of
students enrolled in each type of program. Under this approach, it is important that the financing formula
reflect intrinsic sources of higher costs - such as dispersed population, and a higher-than-average density
of students with specials learning needs. But developing such a formula will require preparation in the
form of a careful examination of the sources and magnitudes of intrinsic cost differences prior to
implementation of the formula. Another problem is that the current financing formula provides virtually
no financing for in-service teacher training. Failure to fund teacher training is a high-risk and low-
efficiency option, because it means that the education system's most important asset - its teachers - are
not equipped to perform their work effectively, especially in introducing the important innovations
planned under the education reform. As a condition of second tranche release, we will adopt an
appropriate legal act to define capitation-basedformulae for calculating categorical grants in the health
and education sector. The new formulae will be implemented before the third tranche release.
58.    Costs of Pre-University Education: In principle, local Governments finance primary and
secondary schools from their own resources. The current practice of financing teachers' salaries under
the Republican budget has the very compelling advantage that it ensures that even the poorest localities
are able to pay teachers' salaries and keep their schools open. But it has a serious disadvantage in that it
does not include financing for textbooks. Textbooks are financed by parents under a textbook rental
scheme. Although rental charges appear modest in an absolute sense, the extremely low level of incomes
means that many households cannot afford the cost of textbook rental, thus preventing children from
having textbooks. This is a major reason for low quality and incomplete school attendance in poor areas
of the country - including many rural areas. By second tranche, we will develop and implement a plan to
modify the textbook rental scheme to ensure that low household income does not prevent students from
receivng textbooks.
59.    Social Protection: Pension reform in the Kyrgyz Republic is at a critical juncture. The recently
completed Public Expenditure Review highlights the need for the Government to clarify priorities among
the multiple objectives of the current pension system, based on the 1997 reforms. Proposals for a new,
fully funded pillar within the Kyrgyz pension system have been advocated because of a concern that
incentives for contributors to participate in the current system are inadequate, and a belief that
introduction of a funded pillar could promote economic growth by raising the level of savings and
encouraging the development of financial and capital markets. The Bank's technical assessment of the
proposal for a fully-funded pillar indicates the need for first investigating the prerequisite costs, and
feasibility of this approach in current Kyrgyz circumstances, before taking any irrevocable steps to create
and implement a funded system. By second tranche, we will identify the prerequisites and necessary
lead-time for adopting a funded approach to pensions by developing an action program and timetable,
satisfactory to IDA, for creating the policies, institutions and systems required to introduce a fully funded
element in to the basic structure of the Kyrgyz pensions system. By third tranche, we will demonstrate
progress, satisfactory to IDA, in implementing an agreed subset of the action plan required to introduce a
fully funded element into the basic structure of the Kyrgyz pensions system.
(vii)  Enhancing the Effectiveness of the Civil Service
60.    Our country is in the initial stages of developing a professional civil service. A Law on Civil
Service (LCS), enacted in 1999, defines the civil service in very broad terms and covers political
appointees, such as the Prime Minister, members of the Government, nominees of the President and



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural A djustment Credit - PF ogram Docement,, April 21, 2003  60
Deputies of the Parliament and their assistants and advisers, as well as career civil servants. Thus, the
line between permanent civil servants and the po litical execLutive is blurred. Political appointments also
extend quite deep down the administrative hierarchy. Changes in political heads of ministries and
agencies, therefore, lead to large scale changes iin incumbents at lower levels. The LCS also provides
inadequate protection to career civil servants against politically motivated dismissals. As a result, the
independence, impartiality and quality of advice provided by them to i:he political executive suffers.
61.    While the LCS and associated regulations have created a basic framework for human resource
management in the civil service. this framework has not been fully institutionalized. In addition, the
current rules need to be improved in several respects. Rernuneration of the civil service, as also of other
public services, remains way below competitive ].evels. Low salaries Lead to low quality of public
services, encourage misuse of regulatory authority and create incentives for corruption. Turnover of staff
at skilled levels is quite high. Although the Government has carried cut retrenchment of staff in the past
years, there still appears to be scope for further rightsizing. VWhile new skills are sorely needed to equip
civil servants to handle the evolving role of the State, no systematic training needs assessment is carried
out, Most training is ad hoc and depends on donor funding. IFinally, the lack of adequate institutional
arrangements for managing the civil service is a major deficiency. The capacity of the Civil Sector
Division in the President's Administration, which performs this function, is extremely limited. The
capacity of the personnel departments of ministries and agencies is similarly constrained.
62.    We are committed to establish a well-qualified, professional civil service, that would assist the
Govemment in formulating and implementing policies for effective governance and poverty reduction.
We have taken various measures in the recent past towards this objective. Merit-based competitive
recruitment was launched in 2001; in order to trim the size of' the civil service 1,200 civil servants were
retrenched in 2001 and 1750 civil servants and managers in budgetary entities are expected to be
retrenched in 2002; a Presidential decree on Ethics of Civil Servants was issued and Ethics Commissions
were created at the level of the President's Administration and other M4inistries and Central agencies. In
continuation of these efforts, we have initiated discussion of draft amendments to the Law on Civil
Service with relevant stakeholders. By second tranche, we will: (i) en2ct amendments to the Law on Civil
Service, satisfactory to IDA, with a view to separate political appoini,ees and career civil servants;
increase competitiveness in recruitment and promotion; reduce political interference in personnel
management decisions; improve incentives for performance and integrity; and improve institutional
arrangements for management of the civil service. By third tranche, we will: (i) establish the Civil
Service Management Agency (CSMA) and ensure that it isfidly opercational, by appointing management
and staff, providing adequate budgetary resources, and deveioping rules and regulations for the
functioning of the agency, In addition, before third tranche, we will take measures to ensure that all civil
servants recruited for the first time to 'main' and 'senior' positions are recruited on the basis of open
competition and merit, in accordance with the Civil Service Law and that all persons promoted to "main"
and "senior" positions are promoted on the basis of results of performance evaluation or attestation.
Further, we will implement a revised remuneration structure for the civil service, satisfactory to IDA,
before the third tranche.
(viii) Streamlining Structure of Government Ministries and Agen cies
63.    Our State, like all other FSU countries, inherited a wide rangt of functions. While its role has
shrunk since then, the span of activities continues to be large relative to its resources and capacity. The
roles and responsibilities of Government at the central, oblasit, rayon and local level are not entirely clear.
At the central Govemment level, there is duplication and olverlapping of functions between different
ministries and between different units, departments and agencies under individual ministries. At the
regional level, there is ambiguity about the authority of the governors and the ministries over subordinate
organizational units and their accountability for service delivery.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  61
64.    In addition to the functional review of the Ministry of the Health mentioned above, the
Government has carried out functional reviews of the organizational structure of the Ministries of
Education and Agriculture and Water Resources, with a view to rationalize the organization structure and
remove duplication of functions. Other ministries and agencies are in the process of carrying out their
own functional reviews, with assistance from DFID and UNDP. By second tranche, we will extend
Functional Reviews of Ministries of Health, Education and Labor and Social Development and one
additional ministry to be agreed with IDA, to cover their local branches and affiliated agencies, such as
the Mandatory Health Insurance Fund. We will also prepare an action plan for rightsizing of staff in these
Ministries and agencies, based on the results of the functional reviews. By third tranche, we will make
satisfactory progress in implementation of recommendations of the functional reviews in the Ministries
of Health, Education and Labor and Social Development and one additional ministry to be agreed with
IDA,, at the central and local levels, and in their affiliated agencies. We will also make satisfactory
progress in implementing the action plan for rightsizing of staff in these ministries and agencies.
Continuing Reforms in the Energy Sectors
65.    In addition to the reforns discussed above, the Government places high priority on the energy
sector, as a means of economic stability and a key source of medium to long-term economic growth for
the Kyrgyz republic for the following reasons:
•  Current economic and financial losses of the sector threaten macroeconomic stability and fiscal
prudence. They also expose unnecessarily the country to changes in weather conditions affecting
electricity and water service demand from neighboring countries-which further complicates the
already difficult challenge of conducting stable macroeconomic policies.
*  Availability of reliable and adequate quantities of electricity is fundamental to other sector of the
economy which are targeted for contributing to economic growth, including agricultural
production, development of mineral resources, development of SMEs, and Information and
Communication Technologies;
*  Electricity itself is the second largest exported commodity, but there is additional significant
potential to further increase pure electricity exports, separately from water release services, and
capitalize on Kyrgyz Republic's rich hydropower base;
*  Electricity sector has the potential to contribute to increase in domestic private sector and SME
activities in small hydro and other renewable energy sources and in energy efficiency services;
*  Lack of the reliability in the system is creating unnecessary social unrest among the population
which do not perceive any benefit from higher electricity bills.
•  Meeting the country's electricity needs in a least cost manner will also de-escalate the regional
tensions arising due to the water-energy nexus.
66.    The Government's energy sector reform  aims at improving the financial condition and overall
efficiency of the companies in the Electricity Supply Industry (ESI) and gas sectors. In addition to
spearheading continued efforts to reduce unacceptably high levels of commercial losses, the Government
will continue implementing deep structural reforms of the energy sector, since these are critical to reduce
its contribution to unsustainably high Quasi-Fiscal Deficit, restore quality of service which is essential to
increase consumers' willingness to pay, and tap its potential as an engine of growth. In particular, the
Government is comrnitted to:
* Attracting experienced and reputable private management and mostly private and non sovereign
guaranteed investment resources; both are critical to achieve a significant and sustainable
reduction of the technical and commercial losses in the sector;



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structral Adjustmen Credii' - Progran Document,, Apnl 21, 2003  62
*   Gradually moving towards tariffs thal: cover long run marginal costs (LRMC) of the electricity
and gas industries; and,
*   Better targeting of the social protection mechanism which is a necessary measure to improve the
coverage of the poorest, given budgetary constraints faced by the Kyrgyz Republic.
67.    To achieve these objectives, Government will pursue t,he program of actions documented in
Government Resolution # 175-r of April 8, 2003..
Prime Minister                                           IN. Tanaev

Kyrgyz RepubElc: Governance Strutdural Adjustment Credit - Programn Document,, April 21, 2003  63
Annex 4
Estimated Costs of Reforms
1.    In the tables below, an attempt has been made to estimate the costs, other than those
covered by the GTAC and other donors, that are likely to be faced by the Government in
effectively implementing the proposed reforms.
2.     The costs related to salary improvements of the civil service and retrenchment of civil
servants, will depend on the policy decisions taken by the government regarding the quantum of
annual salary increases and adjustments in staffing numbers. The details of these costs will be
worked out in the course of development of the remuneration and rightsizing strategies, as a part
of project implementation. However, in order to get a broad sense of these costs, four scenarios
have been presented below, assuming different percentage of salary increases and staff
reductions. It must also be mentioned that available data on the number of civil servants and
salaries and allowances has various deficiencies. Therefore, the costs presented below should be
viewed only as indicative of the order of magnitude.

I.      Scenarios of Estimated Costs of Improvements in Civil Service Remuneration
and Rikhtsizing of the Civil Service
Assumptions:
a       Number of core civil servants in 2003 has been taken as 13,290, as per the model developed by SMEC
International under the PHRD Grant.
*       25% cost of employer's contribution has been added to the base salary.
*       The average severance payment payable to each retrenched employee is assumed to be equal to 3 months
average salary of the relevant grade as prevailing in the year of retrenchment.
*       An unemployment payment equal to 625 soms paid to each retrenched employee for 12 months, has been
added in retrenchment costs.
*       The scenarios below do not take into account costs related to increase in remuneration of other services that
may become necessary for equity reasons.

Annex 5:
Public Service Employment and Pay Issues
1.     This annex provides an overview of civil service employment and pay issues, highlights the
critical linkage with broader systemic issues, and identifies steps that must be taken in order to build a
sound basis for analyzing pay and employment issues as part of overall public administration reform.
A.     STATUS OF GOVERNMENT EMPLOYMENT
2.     According to official data, total budget-financed civilian government employment32 - including
the republic-level and local governments - was 308,597 in 2001. Of this number, approximately 13,00033
are estimated to be civil servants, covered by the current civil service law. Due to data shortcomings,
however, the exact number and location of civil servants throughout the administration are somewhat
unclear. Table I shows the distribution of budget-financed civilian government employees by financing
source, branch of government and sector. Since a number of entities finance staff through so-called
special resources, there may be additional workers who are technically not financed from the budget and
thus excluded from this figure.

the extreme margins - of the appvropriateness of the employment level and, more importantly, of the
government's ability to deliver on its responsibilities. Furthermore, a smaller administration is not
necessarily an indicator of better standing. As countries develop, their governments tend to grow, in part
because they are providing a larger range and number of functions and services.

existing staff may be outdated or inadequate, and long-standing management and administrative practices
often impede effective staff performance.

6.      The size of the civil service is estimated at around 13,000, though as noted above, data on the
number and distribution of civil servants lack sufficient detail. Estimates based on available data show
that about 4% of civil servants work in nministry headquarters, 4% in deconcentrated offices of ministries,
66% in headquarters of subsidiary entities of ministries, and 26% in deconcentrated offices of such
subsidiary entities.
PAY AND WAGE BILL ISSUES
7.      The Kyrgyz Republic has several separate wage systems for various groups of employees
including the civil service, social sectors, central bank, and uniformed personnel. In practice, a large
number of regulations govern each wage system, posing a challenge not only to those charged with
implementing current legislation but also, at the republic: level, to anyone who seeks to oversee
implementation of regulations, develop policy options or analyze their fiscal impacts. In fact, for reasons
that include difficulties with information flow and constraints on technical skills and staff capacity, little
systematic, in-depth analysis of this nature appears to have lbeen done to date.36
STRUCTURE OF CIVIL SERVICE EARNINGS
8.      The civil service is paid according to its own wage scale, established by presidential decree and
last updated in decree 196 of August 1, 2000.37 In addition to base salary, civil servants are eligible for a
wide array of allowances and bonuses that account for a significant proportion - generally 50 to 70% - of
an employee's total earnings. Payment of certain allowances is mandatory while others may be reduced
in amount, depending upon the entity's wage bill allocation."' Job-related non-monetary (in-kind)
benefits do not appear to be a significant issue, at least for regular, non-uniformed staff.
36 Many records are still kept in paper, rather than electionic form. To date, centoal oversight of employment levels has largely been limited to
compiling an annual record (in paper form) that lists a total employment figure foi each entit) without greater detail.
37 The decree details 25 sub-scales for employees throughout the civil service, as currently defined. Prominent republic-level institutions tend to
pay higher salaries, and headquarters offices pay more than deconcentrated offices or territorial bodies.
38 For example, payment of the seniority allowance is mandatory whereas the quarterly bonu, (which is generally considered to be an
entitlement, rather than a real bonus tied to individual performance) can be reduced or suspended as a function of available funds.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003                    73
9.       The system of allowances and bonuses raise numerous concerns. The sheer number of bonuses
and allowances (at least 11) and the fact that each has its own criteria make administering the wage
system burdensome (at the level of individual institutions, subsidiary entities and offices) as well as
making it difficult (at the republic level) to monitor implementation and ensure that funds are allocated as
intended.39 Furthermore, the effectiveness and targeting of these payments is unclear. Several allowances
appear to serve similar purposes and, while the criteria for ceitain allowances (most notably, seniority) are
clear, others involve a significant degree of discretion. Such discretion can easily be abused, particularly
in a country like the Kyrgyz Republic, where managerial accountability is weak. Similarly, with few real
incentives to focus staff on quality performance, and little in the way of meaningful measures of actual
staff performance,40 allowances that ostensibly reward good performance may not actually achieve that
objective.
10.      As figure 5 illustrates, even in much more developed public administrations with a stronger
performance orientation (and, in theory, a greater ability to evaluate performance) it is unusual for
allowances to comprise a large proportion of total earnings.

CIVIL SERVICE EARNINGS
11.      While the civil service wage scale is significantly higher than the tariff net used by the social
sectors (see below), civil service pay levels remain modest at best, and are considered inadequate by most
observers (though, bearing in mind the poverty that afflicts the Kyrgyz Republic, the same might be said
of salaries in other sectors). Based on 2001 data and estimates for nine of 12 ministries,42 one of the
lowest civil service posts (specialist, category 1) in a ministry headquarters earns about 1,960 soms ($40
US) per month, including all allowances and bonuses, while a deputy minister earns about 9,105 soms
($188 US) in total. Table 2 gives an indication of salaries - as well as the importance of allowances - for

12.     Given the underdeveloped nature of the private sector, public-private salary comparisons are
difficult and of limited practical relevance. Regular salary surveys are generally unavailable and, for all
but a few staff with niche skills - lawyers or financial specialists with western expertise -- or special
opportunities, moving to a comparable post outside the administration is not a real option.       While some
individuals in Bishkek may find employmrrent in connectiort with donor projects, the majority of rank and
file staff has little option but to try to make ends meet by minimizing expenditures.
13.     An additional feature of the pay system - less impoitant than earnings levels or structure, but
worth noting nevertheless - is that the salary scale is somevvhat compressed.43 High-ranking ministry staff
at the top of their careers earn perhaps four to five times moire than the most junior civil service position.44
This means that the official salary provides only a moderate incentiva for an individual to take on the
greater responsibilities and workload that are presumably associated with more senior posts.
Civil Service Salaries in Context
14.     National Statistical Committee (NSC) data on earnings by sector as well as the poverty line
provide a context for civil service earnings. As figure 6 indicates, earnings in the state administration
(assumed here to be roughly equivalent to the civil service) would place a sole wage-earner's family
below the poverty line.45 However, the data also show only one sector - finance - as paying average
eamings above the poverty line; and the state administration along with several other sectors shows
markedly higher earnings than the social and agricultural sectors. Furthermore, the state administration
falls into the top 20%  of the population by average monthly earnings (2147 som in 2001).46 Thus while
43 In addition to the base salary scale, some managers report redistributing "extra" wage bill finds (from vacancies or other under-spends) in a
manner that gives preference to lower level staff, who are particularly hard-bit by lDw salaries.

(Ready for Europe: Public Administration Reform and European union Accession [n Central and Eastern Europe, World Bank., 2000).
Cotnpression ratios serve as a rough and easily available indicator of earmings potential, thoug'i they are not as insightful as other, more data-
intensive methodologies.

2001. Family poverty line is calculated from annual per capita poverty line and avvcrage family size data.

many civil servants clearly struggle to make ends meet, they appear - based on this quick analysis -- to be
relatively better off than many other income earners in a country that is, unfortunately, afflicted by high
poverty rates.

15. This is not to dismiss the need for pay reformns. Indeed, there is a strong case for focusing
attention on civil servants who, as the elite administrative backbone of a country, maintain the framework
of the state through important activities such as policy formulation, coordination and implementation.
However, the critical need is for a careful, analytical approach that considers not just appropriate (and
affordable) earnings levels but also systemic problems that include: an opaque, discretionary salary
structure; little ability to ensure that wage funds are in fact spent appropriately; a weak legislative and
institutional framework; work environments fraught with management weaknesses and distorted
incentives - and also takes into account equity issues and the effect of civil service reforms on the much
larger group of public sector employees covered by the tariff net.
SOCIAL SECTOR SALARY SYSTEM
16.      In the social sectors, jobs are slotted into a 23-level tariff net that calculates monthly base salaries
by applying a series of coefficients to a cornerstone amount. Following an April 1, 2002 increase in the
cornerstone, monthly salaries should range from 196 som ($4US) at the bottom to 1120 som ($23) at the
top.47 An individual's total earnings, however, take into account additional factors such as workload and
particular tasks performed.48
WAGE BiLL ISSUES
17.      In 2001, government expenditures on wage and salaries amounted to an estimated 5.8%                of
GDP.49 As with employment numbers, cross-country comparisons of wage expenditures are of limited
47 As an example, a newly-graduated teacher might earn 500 soms ($10). Nation-wide, the average monthly salary for doctors was 985 soms
($20), of which 780 soms were budget-financed and the remainder from insurance payments. Mid-level medical personnel eamed 705 soms
($15), of which 562 soms were budget-financed. The exchange rate used throughout this text is $1 = 48.4 sorns.
48 This system includes work norms -- for blue collar as well as professional jobs - that in some cases have not been updated since the Soviet era.
Mechanistic approaches effectively result in a system that pays far less than a living wage while restricting worker output, rather than allowing
managers to hire a smaller number of productive staff at more acceptable salaries. In the case of professional staff the restrictive nature of the
tariff net and lack of managerial authority over and accountability for budget expenditures have grave implications for service delivery.
49 The actual cost of the government workforce is higher, since some entities use self-generated revenues ("special resources") to finance
additional posts or to supplement budget-funded salaries. An analysis of the 2001 budget execution report shows that 414 million som (0.6%



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance SlructuralAdjustn2ent Credit- Progrrm Docum,ent,, April 21, 2003                    76
use except at the extremes due to the h-igh level of aggregation of such data, concerns regarding data
accuracy, and legitimate variations in country needs and contexts. That said, by this limited measure, the
Kyrgyz Republic's wage bill is not excessive, and even falls at the low end of ranges. Judged by more
meaningful criteria such as the sustainability and effectiveness - in te.rms of administrative performance -
of wage expenditures, however, there is little reason for complacency. Furthermore, since budget flows
in the Kyrgyz Republic often take the form of in-kind transtfrs rather than monetary transactions, the
wage bill takes on added impottance since it is in fact one of the larger monetary flows.
OVERSIGHT OF EMPLOYMENTAND WAGE BI'LL EXPEND[TURES
18.      In the absence of computerized payroll and personncl systems, the Kyrgyz Republic does not
have a robust system for monitoring whether wage bill funds are well spent. Monitoring of employment
expenditures relies on paper submissions (such as detailed accounting forms for individual salary
payments) and rote compliancc checks of paperwork as it gets passed up through the system; these reports
are highly aggregated by the tine ministries submit them to the Ministry of Finance and Treasury to
request transfers of their wage bill allocation. It is difficult to imagiiie how such a system could
safeguard against intentional mis-appropriation of funds or simple errors (such as. recently-discovered
cases of organizations using obsolete wage guidelines).50 WVhile audits by the Chamber of Accounts
might theoretically uncover some problems after the fact., that body presently lacks sufficient staff to
adequately scrutinize personnel expenditures.
19.      Until now, central records of employment levels have consisted primarily of an annual paper-
based report that compiles total employment figures submitted by each organization. Employing
organizations and their subsidiary units do hold more disaggregated (lata, albeit not always in readily
accessible electronic form. However, it had not been systernatically compiled until the Ministry of
Finance in Spring 2002 collected data - albeit covering only headquarters civil service staff -- that details
employment and expenditures down to the level of individual posts and allowances.
20.      Beyond data and record-keeping issues, however, there are questions about appropriate levels of
autonomy over budget management, and incentives for entities to manage their expenditures (and
emnployment) effectively.5'

salaties is financed from special resources. The extent of reliance on these funds varies signific,intly among functions, with little or no salary-
related use of such funds in defense, public order, social protection/social msurance, and mining. In contrast, such financing amounts to 39% of
total salaries in the transport &comnmunications sector, 19% in housing, and 16% in education.

human resource management infornation system (HRMIS) that enables effective management of the workforce.

of Education - to retain a portion of funds thus saved for its own use. Such measures could help institutions focus on attaining a rational

Annex 6
Oualitv of Service and Corruption
Summary of Survey Results
1.     Surveys of households, business enterprises and public officials were conducted with the
assistance of the PHRD Grant in March 2001. The surveys covered 1,000 individuals, 400
enterprise managers and 350 public officials. The results of these surveys are summarized
below.
2.     Service Delivery: Feedback provided by survey respondents indicates considerable
variability in the quality of services provided by the State. Enterprises provided high ratings to
telephone and water utilities and the post office. However, few enterprise managers were
satisfied with the quality of service provided by police, courts, inspection agencies, tax
administration and customs. Households gave high marks to the mass media. However,
education, garbage collection, health services, social protection benefits, railways, police and the
courts had poor satisfaction rates ranging from 22% to 8%. The responses of public servants
broadly mirrored those of enterprises and households.
3.     Corruption: Not unlike some of the other countries of the region, the surveys reveal
widespread corruption in almost all aspects of the State's dealing with firms and individuals.
Table 1 summarizes the responses of the business community and households about the
frequency with which they have to pay bribes and the average amount of the bribe paid. As
would be seen from the table, the percentage of firms who reported encountering corruption in
their interactions with the judiciary, police, traffic police, tax inspectors, customs and registration,
licensing and inspection agencies is quite high. Similarly, a large percentage of households report
having to pay bribes while coming in contact with courts and traffic police, or in the course of
using public services relating to health care, education, social assistance, veterinary services and
agricultural services. Even public officials confirmed the existence of corruption in some aspects
of public sector functioning. One in five public officials familiar with procurement practices in
their institutions said that unofficial payments are frequently made to gain contracts in public
tenders. Further, 17% of the surveyed public officials said that embezzlement of public funds
sometimes occurs where they work, and 7 percent said that it happens very often.
4.     While Table 1 indicates systemic administrative corruption, the surveys also provide
some evidence of State capture. One in four firms said that they are significantly affected by
corruption in enactment of laws and decrees. Seventeen percent of the elected officials that
participated in the public officials survey said they had been offered money or an expensive
present in the 12 months prior to the surveys. One in nine public officials said that their
institution does accept bribes for granting monopoly rights from time to time.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Struitural Adjustnent Credit - Prograrn L)ocument,, April 21, 2003  78
