Annex 7
Reforms Proposed in the National Poverty Reduction Strategy for
the Formation of an Effective State and
their Linkage to the GSA C/GTA C
1.     The National Poverty Reduction Strategy (NSPR) covers eight areas related to
govemance and public sector management, namely, (a) democratic institutions, (b) the
legislature; (c) the judiciary and law enforcement agencies; (d) central and local public
administration; (e) decentralization and local self-government; (f) state financial management; (g)
civil service; and (h) electronic government. In each of these areas, the NSPR discusses the
recent achievements of the government, the continuing problems and proposed actions for the
future.
2.     Democratic Institutions: With the holding of elections for Local Self Govemment
(LSG) in all localities of the country in 2001, the electoral system has expanded significantly. A
mechanism of partial elections by party lists has been introduced in the national parliament,
residence qualifications have been established for deputies of the Assembly of the People's
Representatives and immunity of parliamentarians against criminal prosecution has been lifted.
To stimulate the involvement of the people in national development, people's conventions were
held throughout the country in 2001 and extensive consultations were carried out at the
community level in the context of the CDF/ NSPR. Local associations of user of local services
have been formed. There are more than 3.500 registered NGOs, political parties and movements.
A National Human Rights Program has been initiated.
3.     The main problems in democratization are continuing weaknesses in the electoral system,
weak political parties, instances of violation of constitutional and human rights of the people by
state agencies and officials, inequities in access to public infrastructure and services, and lack of
effective communication between the state and the people. The proposed strategies include
improving the electoral system, strengthening of political parties, enhancing civil control over the
military and law enforcement agencies, increasing transparency in the distributive functions of
the state, capacity building of LSGs, promoting community associations and trade unions,
empowering citizens through increased access to informnation about the operation of the state,
increasing consultation between the government and the community and civil society,
strengthening community control over state and local budgets, protecting the rights of ethnic
minorities and safeguarding religious rights, while resisting religious extremism.
4.     Raising the effectiveness of the legislature: This section essentially pertains to legal
reform. In the last 11 years more that 1,100 laws have been passed. However, many of the laws
and regulations are outdated, conflict with treaty obligations, or are inconsistent with each other.
There is a lack of a well-established legislative process. Many laws are declarative in nature,
without clear implementation mechanisms. No systematic analysis of the impact of proposed
laws is carried out. Laws change too frequently, creating confusion for the public and the private
sector. The law making process does not provide for obtaining feedback from the people and
civil society. The public has limited access to existing legal and normative acts.
5.     The proposed strategy includes measures designed to streamline the legislative process
through review and improvement of existing legislation, improved coordination between different
state bodies engaged in legislation, enabling the public to comment on draft laws and regulations,



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Struactural Adjustment Credit -- Progrant Document,, April 21, 2003  80
increasing public access to legislation, requiring assessment of impact of proposed legislation
before it is enacted and strengthening the capacity of the parliament.
6.     Reforming the Judiciary and Law Enforcement Agencies: Various laws have been
passed in recent years to create the institutional framework for the judiciary. A training center
for judges has been established and salaries of judges have been inicreased. Also Arbitration
Courts have been set up and are being increasingly resorted to. Congresses of Judges and
Barristers have been held to consider issues relating to the rule of law. However, serious
problems weaken the rule oF law. These include corruption and professional incompetence in law
enforcement agencies and the judiciary, leading to lack oF public trust in these institutions; lack of
access to justice to the poor and lack of transparency in judicial decision making. The NSPR
strategy consists of an extensive set of actions to strengthen the institutional capacity of the
judiciary, introduction of the principle of election of judges, involvement of direct participation of
people's representatives in the administration of j ustice, ensuring access to justice to all,
provision of legal aid to the poor, reduction in the duration of court proceedings, strengthening
control of citizens on activities of judges, strengthening imnplemerntation of court decisions, and
promotion of alternate dispute resolution mechanisms. WTith regard to law enforcement agencies,
the strategy envisages strengthening their capacity to fight crime amd corruption, including
introduction of measures to reduce corruption within these agencies themselves, and improving
prison administration. There is also a proposal to strengthen the Blar.
7.     Reforming Central and Local Public Administration: The government has taken
various measures in the last few years to streamnline the structure of the government at the central
and regional levels and reduce staff. Functional reviews of three ministries have been completed
and of other ministries and agencies are underway. Inter-department groups and commissions,
including NGOs representatives are being widely used tO develop national, regional and sector
programs and review draft laws. However, various issues remain. The policy making capacity of
the public sector is weak; implementation of policies is unleven; new management systems
attuned to the emerging role of the public sector have yet to develop; there is inadequate
transparency and accountability in the functioning of the state; and there is undue government
interference in business activities. The strategy for the future includes clarification of the roles
and responsibilities of executive authorities are various levels, optimization of the structures of
public entities, improving accountability, openness and transparency, reallocating regulatory
functions between agencies with privatization of some of these, intensifying the fight against
corruption and developing staff capacity.
8.     Decentralization and Local Self Governance: The Local Self Government elections
were a major step in the decentralization process. A Law on LSG and local state administration
was passed in 2002. There are now 487 LSGs at village and city level. The Law on the Courts of
the Elderly promotes resolution of disputes locally. Public hearings on local budgets have been
introduced. Additional powers have been transferred to L.SGs from the Ministry of Labor and
Social Protection. LSGs have also been given greater authority in budgeting and local taxation
and communal property has been transferred to them. Continuing problems in LSGs include
weak legal framework, need for additional delegation of a,uthority., continuing centralization of a
significant part of the local budget, mismatch between mandates and financial resources and lack
of managerial, administrative and technical capacity. The strateg) to strengthen local self
government includes improvement in legislation, increased fiscal dlevolution, improvement in
asset management, enhancement of capacity of municipal civil servants and building civil society
partnerships.
9.     Reforming State Financial Management: This section does not discuss the first
generation reforms in public expenditure management that have already been implemented in



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit- Program Document,, April 21, 2003  81
recent years. The important deficiencies highlighted are the need to improve budget formulation
to ensure financing of priority areas under the NSPR, grant greater autonomy to ministries,
departments and regions in budget management, increase predictability of budget flows and
increase transparency, accountability and timeliness in budget execution. The strategy to
strengthen PEM include improving macro-economic forecasting, strengthening project planning
and prioritization capacity, improving budget formulation through the gradual introduction of a
Medium Term Budget Framework and program budgeting, improving the budget code,
computerization of the Treasury and restructuring of the Ministry of Finance.
10.    Creating an Effective Civil Service: A Civil Service Law was passed in 1999. Since
then a register of civil service positions has been developed, regulations on attestation, grading
and promotion of civil servants and creation of a civil service reserve have been developed and
civil service salaries have been raised. A Code of Ethics of Civil Servants has been instituted.
Ethics Commissions have been set up at the central, regional and local levels. However, reforms
in the the civil service system need to be deepened. There is a lack of clear differentiation
between political and career positions, civil servants have inadequate protection against dismissal
for political reasons, the level of civil service skills is low, especially in policy making and
implementation areas that are key to poverty reduction, salaries are inadequate, organizational
arrangements for management of the civil service are weak and there is a lack of a performance
and client service orientation. The reform strategy comprises of improvements in the civil service
law and regulations, strengthening civil service management, implementing a medium term
strategy to adjust the size of the civil service and improve its remuneration, and upgrading skills
in critical areas.
11.   Electronic Government: Initial steps to increase the use of the Information and
Communication Technologies (ICT) in govemment have been taken with the creation of the State
Computer Network and training of senior executives in use of computers. Also web-pages of
some of the main state organs have been created. The future strategy includes the development of
a system to monitor the implementation of the CDF and NSPR. In addition, it includes an
ambitious vision of re-engineering the structure and business processes of the whole public
administration and development of a National Information Network that would improve
efficiency and cost effectiveness of public sector operations, provide real- time information for
decision making and facilitate communication with the public.
12.   The GSAC/GTAC would help the government in meeting some of the important strategic
objectives included in the NPRS. These are:
*  Increasing the dissemination of information about important aspects of the functioning of the
public sector to citizens and civil society, to strengthen transparency, accountability and
participation.
* Enhancing transparency in the process of development of legal and normative acts, to ensure
stakeholder inputs and improve consistency.
* Strengthening of grievance and appellate mechanisms, to provide recourse against
administrative excesses.
*  Reducing perverse incentives of regulatory agencies through elimination of 'special means'
funding derived from regulatory activities.
*  Strengthening extemal and internal audit systems.
* Improving public expenditure management through improvements in budget formulation,
budget execution and the structure of the Ministry of Finance.
* Rationalizing the structure of the government to reduce functional duplication and improve
efficiency.



Kyrgyz Republic: Governance Structural Adjushnent Credit- Programn Document,, April 21, 2003  82
* Improving the professionalism and capacity of the civil service, to formulate and execute
policies aimed at sustainable growth and poverty reduction.
13.     In addition to the above, we have also included in the GSAC agenda improvement in the
system of declaration of assets and incomes and public procuremnnt. Almost all the interventions
under the GSAC/GTAC would directly and indirectly contribute to the fight against corruption.



Kyrgyz RepublUc: Governance Structural Adjustment Credit - Program Document,, April 21, 2003  83
Annex 8
Development of a Communications Strateny to Support the Reform ARenda
1.     The GSAC and GTAC    operations aim at improving transparency of State affairs and
govemment accountability to stakeholders. To achieve these objectives, it will be necessary to
increase public access to relevant information about public sector reform through effective
communications channels. This will ensure that the public has the necessary information and the
means to act as an effective enforcer of good government.
2.     At the same time, key groups within the administration of the State are expected to
participate actively in the implementation of reforms under GSAC and GTAC in order to make
these operations successful. Many of these reforms imply a drastic change in the status quo (i.e.
publication of tax declarations of high state officials, enacting of a new Civil Service Law, greater
attention on public grievances regarding performance of public institutions, etc.) Identifying these
groups' perceptions of project components affecting them, positively or negatively, will help
pinpoint possible roadblocks to project implementation early on and map out mitigation activities.
3.    In collaboration with ECA External Affairs, a Needs Assessment relating to
communication relating to the GSAC and GTAC would first be developed. The objective of this
exercise will be to identify opportunities and obstacles that may affect smooth design and
implementation of project activities. Once the needs assessment is completed, an Action Plan will
set out short-, medium- and long-term objectives. It will also map out communications activities
and a timeframe for implementation.
4.     A preliminary review of the activities to be implemented under GSAC and GTAC
suggests the need to focus support on three areas: i) overcoming possible obstacles to
implementation by addressing the risk of vested interest opposition early on; ii) strengthening
public access to relevant information on State affairs, and iii) creating public demand for public
sector reforms.
I.     Overcoming obstacles to project implementation:
5.     Activities under this focal area would be geared towards identifying political, cultural and
social roadblocks to the successful preparation/implementation of program activities in order to
map out specific strategies to overcome them. Efforts will also aim at identifying constituents
that strongly support this operation and developing mechanisms to consolidate and echo this
support.
6.     Perceptions of main stakeholders affected by the project, positively or negatively will be
identified during the needs assessment. This analysis will include the following groups:
+ Civil servants & public officials
+ Parliamentarians
+ Officials in the ministries that will be reorganized under the project (i.e. Ministry of Health)
+ Teachers and other education professionals
+ Procurement staff in line ministries
+ Chambers of Account
+ Users of public services



Kyrgyz Republic: Governane StructuralAdjustment Credit- Program Document,, April 21,2003  84
7.     Based on this analysis, activities to overcome potential roadblocks and enhance public
support for public reforms will be included in a communications strategy in support of the GSAC
and GTAC operations.
II.    Strengthening public access to relevant infomlnation on State affairs:
8.     Activities under this focal area would be geared towards putting in place the right
communications mechanisms to ensure availability of relevant informnation on State affairs and
public sector reforms.
9.     Communications activities will help define which segments of civil society are more
active in promoting State tiansparency and therefore more willing to act as "enforcers" of good
practice in this domain (i.e. chambers of commerce, NGOs, AcacLemia, etc.) Although
communications efforts should ensure that information on State affairs is accessible to the general
population, this group would constitute the main target audience for this kind of information flow.
10.    Once the main audience has been identified, the most effective communications channels
to reach them will be selected.
11.    More specifically, communications activities will focus on increasing public access to the
following data:
+ status of public sector reform under GSAC and GTAC
+ declarations of income and assets of high State officials
+ audit reports
+ new legislation and draft laws
+ procurement process (including publication of contraci awards)
+ process of developing and implementing naticnal and r egional budgets
+ progress on health reform
+ public perceptions of the performance of selected government agencies
12.    Staff at the Govemnment Public Infornation Center (GPIC) which is to be established
under the project would be responsible for all issues related to the provision of information on
State Affairs. It will also act as a conduit for bottom-up information flows.
III.   Creating public demand for public sector reforms:
13.    Efforts will also be geared towards creating public demand for good govenmment by
implementing communications strategies that build credibility for the reforms to be carried out
under the GSAC and GTAC operations. These will be based on public opinion data that identifies
public expectations and concerns regarding public sector performance and looking at the fit
between these concerns and the GSAC and GTAC refon-n activities.
14.    Communications activities will also aim at enabling public consultations on government
policy, draft laws and regulations; and supporting the creation of effective public grievance
mechanisms to create two-way communications channels and st-engthen public participation in
government decision-making.
15.    By being able to effectively communicate with civil society on key areas of public sector
reform, the government will build public understanding of its program which is necessary to
secure comrnitment to refonns on the part of all stakeho]lders. This approach will also contribute
to promote transparency in the govemment, integrity in public sector activities, and a culture of
professional ethics across public institutions.
